The Obligation to Affirm God's Perfect Attributes and the Name al-Jalil
Sanusi concludes the proof of negative attributes and defines the divine name al-Jalil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical consequence of the impossibility of temporal origination for Allah, the obligatory belief in affirming His befitting attributes and negating opposites, and the meaning of the divine name al-Jalil.

Core Idea
Knowing Allah's impossibility of temporal origination compels affirming His perfect attributes and negating their opposites.

Having proven that temporal origination (huduth) is impossible for Allah, Sanusi now draws the practical conclusion: the very same reasoning that negates imperfection establishes that Allah must be described with what befits His majesty. He then begins to unfold the divine names, starting with al-Jalil, whose meaning humbles even the mightiest.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Let the logical flow from proof to obligation to name sink in.
2
Trace the logical connection
Identify why the impossibility of huduth leads to affirming the mentioned attributes. Notice the word 'so' (fa) indicating consequence.
The passage says 'when the impossibility of huduth is established, His being described with the mentioned attributes is established.'
3
Study the name al-Jalil
Read the explanation of al-Jalil: the Great in station, before whose majesty tyrants submit. Reflect on the response of the knowers (arifun).
This name teaches us awe and humility before Allah's majesty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَهو الجليلُ والْجَمِيل والوَلي * والطَّاهِرُ القَدُّوسُ والرَّبُّ العَلِي | ٣٠
أي: وإذا ثبت استحالة الحدوث عليه تعالی ثبت اتصافه تعالى بالصفات المذكورة، التي يفيد مفهومها السلب عما لا يليق بالذات العلية اتصافها به.
فالواجب على كلِّ مكلَّف أن يعتقد اتصاف الرب تَبَارَكَ وَتَعالى بما يليق به من الصفات العُلى، ونفي أضدادها، والله يهدي إلى الحق ويهدي إلى سواء السبيل.
إذا علمت ما ذُكر (فهو) سُبْحَانَهُ وَتَعَالَى (الجليل): العظيم الشأن الذي خضعت لجلالته رقاب الجبابرة، ولذلك ترى العارفين من هيبته وجلال عظمته خاشعين.
So He is the Majestic, the Beautiful, the Protecting Friend, the Pure, the Holy, the Lord, the Most High. | 30. That is, when the impossibility of temporal origination (huduth) for Him, Exalted, is established, His being described with the mentioned attributes is established, the concept of which negates what is unfit for the Most High Essence to be described with. So it is obligatory upon every legally responsible person (mukallaf) to believe that the Lord, Blessed and Exalted, is described with what befits Him of the most high attributes, and to negate their opposites. And Allah guides to the truth and guides to the straight path. When you know what has been mentioned, (So He) Glorified and Exalted (is al-Jalil): the Great in station, before whose majesty the necks of tyrants have submitted, and therefore you see the knowers (arifun) humbled out of fear of His awe and the majesty of His greatness.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a conclusion and a beginning of the exposition of divine names. First, the commentator draws the logical consequence: since huduth is impossible for God, His being described with the negative attributes (that negate anything unworthy) is established. Then he states a theological duty: every responsible believer must believe that God is described with what befits Him of the high attributes and must negate their opposites. This is the practical upshot of the previous proof. Then, the commentator begins to explain the names mentioned in line 30 of the poem: 'So He is al-Jalil, al-Jamil, al-Wali, at-Tahir, al-Quddus, ar-Rabb, al-Ali.' The OCR provides only the explanation of al-Jalil: it means the One of great majesty, before Whose glory the necks of tyrants are subdued, and the knowers are humbled out of awe of His magnificence. This begins the section on the beautiful names, showing that each name is derived from an attribute that befits God.

Key Terms
مكلف
صفات العلى
الجليل
العارفين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The names of God are mere titles without theological content.
Correction: Each name reflects a real attribute and implies a duty to affirm what befits God and negate its opposite.

Checkpoint
What is the obligatory belief regarding the attributes of God according to this passage?
A) To affirm all attributes as equal to human attributes
B) To believe that God is described with what befits Him of high attributes and to negate their opposites
C) To deny all attributes to avoid anthropomorphism
D) To accept only the attributes explicitly mentioned in the Qur'an

Key Takeaway
The proof of Allah's transcendence leads directly to the duty of affirming His beautiful names and negating their opposites, with al-Jalil teaching us awe and humility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah is described with a majesty that subdues tyrants? How does this belief impact the way you approach Him in your daily prayers, if at all? What would change if you truly felt His majesty?
?
The passage says every mukallaf must negate the opposites of Allah's attributes. What opposite of an attribute do you find yourself unconsciously attributing to Allah? (e.g., thinking He is unaware of your struggles, or that He is weak to help).
?
You claim to be among the knowers (arifun) – but are you humbled out of awe? Or do you go through religious motions without fear of His majesty? What would it take for you to be truly humbled before Allah tonight?

The Seven Divine Names: Majestic, Beautiful, Pure, and Exalted
Commentary explains seven divine names from the poem.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of seven divine names and how each expresses God's transcendence and evokes a response in the knowers.

Core Idea
God's names reveal His majesty and beauty, inspiring awe and love.

Having established God's attributes of negation, the commentary now explains seven beautiful names from the poem. Each name highlights a different aspect of God's perfection and shows how the truly knowing respond. This lesson walks through each name and its spiritual effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and the verse line
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the verse line that lists all seven names. This will give you a bird's-eye view before diving into each name's explanation.
The verse line is: 'فهو الجليلُ والْجَمِيل والولي * والطَّاهِرُ القَدُّوسُ والرَّبُّ العَلِي'
2
Examine the first two names: al-Jalil and al-Jamal
Focus on al-Jalil (the Majestic) and al-Jamal (the Beautiful). Note how each name affects the knowers: al-Jalil causes humble awe, while al-Jamal causes bewildered infatuation.
These two names balance fear and love.
3
Understand the names of purity: al-Wali, al-Tahir, al-Quddus
See how al-Wali (the Guardian) and al-Tahir (the Pure) are explained together. Then understand al-Quddus (the Holy) as a superlative form of purity.
These names emphasize God's transcendence from anything unbefitting.
4
Reflect on al-Rabb and al-Ali
Consider al-Rabb (the Lord) as the true owner and nurturer, and note that it can be used metaphorically for others. Finally, al-Ali (the Exalted) points to God's elevated rank.
These names highlight God's authority and highness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا علمت ما ذُكر (فهو) سُبْحَانَهُ وَتَعَالَى (الجليل): العظيم الشأن الذي خضعت لجلالته رقاب الجبابرة، ولذلك ترى العارفين من هيبته وجلال عظمته خاشعين.
فَهو الجليلُ والْجَمِيل والولي * والطَّاهِرُ القَدُّوسُ والرَّبُّ العَلِي
(والجميل) المتصف بصفات الجمال والكمال، من علم، وحياة، وقدرة، وإرادة وغيرها، ولذا ترى العارفين من عظيم جماله مولهين وهائمين. (والولي، والطاهر) المنزه عن كل ما لا يليق به (القدوس) القُدْس، وهو الطهر، أي التنزيه العظيم، وهو أبلغ في الطهارة، (والرب) المالك حقيقة، ومربي خلقه شيئًا فشيئًا إلى الحد الذي أراده، ولا يطلق على غيره تعالى مُنكَرًا إلا على سبيل التجوز، (العلي) أي: المرتفع القدر.
When you know what was mentioned, then He, Glorified and Exalted, is al-Jalil: the Mighty in status, before whose majesty the necks of tyrants have bowed, and therefore you see the knowers humble out of awe of His awe and the majesty of His greatness. So He is al-Jalil, al-Jamal, al-Wali, and al-Tahir, al-Quddus, al-Rabb, al-Ali. (Al-Jamal) is the One described with attributes of beauty and perfection, such as knowledge, life, power, will, and others; hence you see the knowers bewildered and infatuated by His immense beauty. (Al-Wali, and al-Tahir) is the One purified from everything unbefitting. (Al-Quddus) derives from quds, which is purity, i.e., the great transcendence, and it is more emphatic in purity. (Al-Rabb) is the true Owner, and the Nurturer of His creation gradually to the limit He wills; it is not applied to other than Him (Exalted) in an absolute sense except metaphorically. (Al-Ali) is the One of elevated rank.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of seven divine names from the poem. It begins by linking to the previous proof: after establishing God's attributes of negation, the author explains that God is 'al-Jalil' (the Majestic), whose majesty causes even tyrants to submit, and the knowers (ʿārifūn) are humble out of awe. Then the verse line is quoted, listing the names. The commentary explains each: 'al-Jamal' (the Beautiful) is described with attributes of beauty and perfection such as knowledge, life, power, and will, causing the knowers to be infatuated. 'al-Wali' (the Ally/Guardian) and 'al-Tahir' (the Pure) are explained together as the One purified from all that is unbefitting. 'al-Quddus' (the Holy) is from 'quds' meaning purity, and it is an emphatic form indicating supreme transcendence. 'al-Rabb' (the Lord) is the true owner and nurturer of creation gradually, and cannot be applied to others in an unqualified sense except metaphorically. 'al-Ali' (the Exalted) is the One of elevated rank. The commentary shows how each name evokes a specific response in the knower: awe, love, or recognition of transcendence.

Correction: The commentary clarifies that 'al-Rabb' is the true Owner and Nurturer, and it can be applied to others metaphorically, though not in an absolute sense.

Commentary

This passage is a definitional explanation of the divine names in the poem.

Introduction to the Seven Attributes of Meanings (Sifat al-Ma'ani)
Al-Mashat introduces the seven attributes of meanings and defines the technical term.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition and significance of the seven attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani) in Islamic theology, how they relate to essential and existential attributes, and the role of ta'wil in interpreting anthropomorphic expressions.

Core Idea
Sifat al-ma'ani are real, distinct attributes that describe Allah's essence.

This passage marks a shift from discussing negative attributes to the affirmative attributes of meanings. Al-Mashat explains that these are called essential and existential attributes and that they number seven. He then provides a technical definition and addresses the necessity of interpretation (ta'wil) for certain verses. The footnote clarifies the precise meaning in theological terminology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation

The passage includes a verse line and commentary.

(٢) ومعناها في اصطلاح المتكلمين كل صفة قائمة بموصوف زائدة على الذات، موجبة لها حكما.
Then the attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani) are seven: i.e., His encompassing knowledge of things. The commentary of Shaykh Hasan al-Mashat: Then indeed these names that the author mentioned—al-Jalil, al-Jamil, al-Wali—are among the names of Allah, the number of which reaches ninety-nine, as in the authentic hadith that whoever enumerates them enters Paradise. He mentioned them as a derivation from what he presented of the negative attributes (sifat al-sulub), since they indicate transcendence (tanzih) and perfection for Allah, as you know. When the author finished that, he began to explain the attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani), which are called essential attributes (sifat dhatiyya) because they do not separate from the essence, and existential attributes (sifat wujudiyya) because they are realized in themselves. He said: 'Then the attributes of meanings that you must know are seven.' The attributes of meanings are true, invalidating the doctrine of the misguided and guiding the deficient, for they interpreted 'hand' as power, 'face' as essence, and 'istiwa'' as domination, going so far as to say that the pause in the verse is on 'and those firmly rooted in knowledge.' The conclusion is that interpretation (ta'wil) is necessary, i.e., carrying the word to other than its apparent meaning, except that the successors (khalaf) specified interpretations, so their interpretation is detailed, while the interpretation of the predecessors (salaf) is general. (1) Narrated by al-Bukhari (2736) and Muslim (2677) in their Sahihs from Abu Hurayra, may Allah be pleased with him, that the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, said: 'Indeed, Allah has ninety-nine names, one hundred minus one; whoever enumerates them enters Paradise.' (2) And its meaning in the terminology of the theologians (mutakallimun) is: every attribute subsisting in a described entity, additional to the essence, entailing a ruling for it.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage introduces the seven attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani) and provides a definition. The commentator begins by noting that the author previously mentioned some divine names (al-Jalil, al-Jamil, al-Wali) among the ninety-nine names, and these names are derived from the negative attributes (sifat al-sulub) because they indicate transcendence and perfection. Then the author transitions to the attributes of meanings, which are also called essential attributes (sifat dhatiyya) because they are inseparable from the essence, and existential attributes (sifat wujudiyya) because they are inherently realized. The commentator states that the author says: 'Then the attributes of meanings that you must know are seven.' He then explains that these attributes are true and serve to invalidate the doctrines of the misguided and to guide the deficient. The misguided ones had interpreted anthropomorphic expressions like 'hand' as power, 'face' as essence, and 'istiwa'' as domination, claiming that the pause in the Quranic verse (3:7) is after 'and those firmly rooted in knowledge,' implying that they know the interpretation. The commentator says that interpretation (ta'wil) is necessary, meaning carrying words away from their apparent meaning. The successors (khalaf) gave detailed interpretations, while the predecessors (salaf) gave general interpretations. The footnote (1) cites the hadith on the ninety-nine names. Footnote (2) defines sifat al-ma'ani in the terminology of the theologians: 'Every attribute subsisting in a described entity, additional to the essence, entailing a ruling for it.' This definition clarifies that these attributes are real and distinct from the essence, yet not separate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that anthropomorphic expressions in the Quran should be taken literally without ta'wil, leading to anthropomorphism.
Correction: The Ahl al-Sunna hold that these expressions require interpretation (ta'wil) to avoid corporealism, while affirming that the attributes of meanings are real and distinct from the essence.

Key Takeaway
The seven attributes of meanings are real, essential, and existential attributes of Allah, defined as attributes subsisting in the essence, additional to it, entailing a ruling.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The definition of sifat al-ma'ani says they are 'additional to the essence.' Does this challenge your intuitive belief that Allah is simple and undivided? How can you embrace this complexity without falling into anthropomorphism or sheer negation?

The First Four Attributes of Meanings and Universal Divine Will
Defines 'attributes of meanings' and explains the first four attributes with universal will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of 'attributes of meanings' and the nature of the first four divine attributes: Knowledge, Life, Power, and Will, including the scope of God's will over all existence.

Core Idea
God's seven 'attributes of meanings' are real qualities added to His essence, each producing a specific effect.

The author begins by defining the technical term 'attributes of meanings' (sifat al-ma'ani) used by theologians to refer to seven essential divine attributes. Then he explains the first four—Knowledge, Life, Power, and Will—and affirms that everything that occurs does so by God's will. This passage establishes a key distinction between attributes that are additional to the essence and mere names.

Guided Path
1
Understand the technical definition
Read the opening sentence defining attributes of meanings. Focus on the three conditions: subsisting in the described, additional to essence, necessitating a ruling.
This definition separates real attributes from mere names.
2
Examine the first four attributes
Go through each attribute: Knowledge, Life, Power, Will. Note how each is defined as an eternal attribute with a specific function.
Each attribute is distinct and additional to the essence.
3
Reflect on the universal will statement
The passage concludes that everything that exists is willed by God. Consider the theological implications.
This affirms God's absolute sovereignty.

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'attributes of meanings' (sifat al-ma'ani) and then explains the first four of these seven attributes as mentioned by the poet (al-nazim). It begins with a technical definition from the theologians: an attribute of meaning is a quality that subsists in the described entity (e.g., God) and is additional to the essence, and it necessitates a specific ruling. Then it quotes the poetic line stating that the attributes of meanings are seven, starting with 'His knowledge encompassing things.' The commentary then elaborates: the first attribute is Knowledge (al-'ilm), which is defined as an eternal attribute by which all things—necessary, contingent, impossible, universal, and particular—are disclosed in detail without any possibility of error. The second attribute is Life (al-hayat), an eternal attribute that makes knowledge and will possible. The third is Power (al-qudra), an eternal attribute by which God creates and annihilates contingent things. The fourth is Will (al-irada), an eternal attribute that specifies which possibilities become actual. The passage then addresses a related issue: everything that exists occurs only because God wills it. This includes both what He commands (like the faith of Abu Bakr) and what He does not command—all is under His will. The explanation follows the order of the poem, first defining the term, then clarifying each attribute, and finally asserting the comprehensive nature of divine will.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's attributes are just names and not real qualities additional to His essence.
Correction: The passage defines attributes of meanings as 'additional to the essence' (زائدة على الذات), meaning they are distinct, real qualities that exist in God and produce effects.

Key Takeaway
The first four attributes of meanings—Knowledge, Life, Power, and Will—are eternal, real qualities of God, and everything that exists does so by His will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's knowledge encompasses every detail of your life, including your secret thoughts and future failures, does that thought make you more mindful of Him or more anxious? What one hidden thought today would you feel ashamed to have exposed?
?

?
The attribute of will specifies possibilities. When you make a choice today, do you pause to recognize that your very ability to choose is only because God willed it? How might that awareness change your next decision?

The Eternal and All-Encompassing Nature of Divine Knowledge
The author defines divine knowledge as an eternal attribute of comprehensive disclosure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine knowledge, according to the Ashʿarī tradition, is an eternal attribute that comprehensively discloses all things—existent and non-existent—with absolute certainty.

Core Idea
Divine knowledge is eternal, infinite, and covers everything without error.

Before we explore the other attributes, we must first understand the foundation: divine knowledge. The author here defines al-ʿilm as the first of the seven meaningful attributes. This passage not only gives its scope—encompassing all things necessary, contingent, impossible, universal, and particular—but also provides a precise formal definition that distinguishes divine knowledge from human knowledge. Let's read it carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the shift from the poet's line to the commentator's explanation.
The poet's line 'thumma al-maʿānī sabʿatun li-l-rāʾī' is a reference to the seven attributes.
2
Identify the scope of divine knowledge
The commentator says divine knowledge encompasses all things: necessary, contingent, impossible, universal, and particular, in detail. Ask yourself: why does it include the impossible?
This shows that God's knowledge is not limited to what exists; it knows all possibilities and impossibilities.
3
Analyze the formal definition
The definition states: 'an eternal attribute by which existent and non-existent things are disclosed as they are, a disclosure that does not admit the opposite in any way.' Break down each part: eternal attribute, disclosure of existents and non-existents, certainty (no opposite).
The phrase 'lā yaḥtamilu al-naqīḍa' means this knowledge is infallible and perfect.
4
Contrast with human knowledge
Reflect on how human knowledge is acquired, limited, and subject to error. Then see how divine knowledge is the opposite: eternal, comprehensive, and certain.
This contrast helps emphasize the absolute perfection of God.

ثُمَّ الْمَعَانِي سَبْعَةٌ للرَّائِي * أي عِلْمُهُ الْمُحِيطُ بالأَشْيَاءِ. وَالْمُرَادُ بِالْمَعَانِي الصِّفَاتُ الْمَعْنَوِيَّةُ. قَالَ الشَّارِحُ: يَعْنِي أَنَّ الصِّفَاتِ الْمَعْنَوِيَّةَ سَبْعَةٌ، أَوَّلُهَا الْعِلْمُ. وَقَوْلُهُ: «أَيْ عِلْمُهُ» أَيْ عِلْمُ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى الْمُحِيطُ بِجَمِيعِ الْأَشْيَاءِ وَاجِبِهَا وَجَائِزِهَا وَمُسْتَحِيلِهَا وَكُلِّيِّهَا وَجُزْئِيِّهَا عَلَى سَبِيلِ التَّفْصِيلِ. وَعَرَّفُوا الْعِلْمَ بِأَنَّهُ: صِفَةٌ أَزَلِيَّةٌ تُنْكَشِفُ بِهَا الْمَوْجُودَاتُ وَالْمَعْدُومَاتُ عَلَى مَا هِيَ عَلَيْهِ، انْكِشَافًا لَا يَحْتَمِلُ النَّقِيضَ بِوَجْهٍ مَا.
Then the [seven] attributes are seven for the seer: i.e. His all-encompassing knowledge. And what is meant by 'the attributes' are the meaningful attributes. The commentator said: He means that the meaningful attributes are seven, the first of which is knowledge. And his saying: 'i.e. His knowledge' means the knowledge of Allah Most High that encompasses all things, necessary, contingent, impossible, universal, and particular, in detail. And they defined knowledge as: an eternal attribute by which existent and non-existent things are disclosed as they are, a disclosure that does not admit the opposite in any way.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the first of the seven meaningful attributes (al-maʿānī), which is divine knowledge (al-ʿilm). The author begins by glossing the matn line 'then the [seven] attributes are seven for the seer' (thumma al-maʿānī sabʿatun li-l-rāʾī) and quotes the interpretation: 'His all-encompassing knowledge' (ay ʿilmuhu al-muḥīṭ bi-l-ashyāʾ). The commentary explains that the 'meaningful attributes' (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya) are seven, and knowledge is the first. It then expands on the scope of divine knowledge: it encompasses absolutely everything—necessary, contingent, impossible, universal, and particular—in full detail (ʿalā sabīl al-tafṣīl). Finally, the passage gives the standard Ashʿarī definition of knowledge: an eternal attribute (ṣifat azaliyya) by which existent and non-existent things are disclosed (tunkashifu bihā al-mawjūdāt wa-l-maʿdūmāt) as they really are, a disclosure that admits no possibility of the opposite (lā yaḥtamilu al-naqīḍa bi-wajhin mā). This definition emphasizes the infallible and comprehensive nature of divine knowledge.

Key Terms
المعاني
العلم
صفة أزلية
انكشاف لا يحتمل النقيض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That divine knowledge is like human knowledge, limited to actual existents only.
Correction: Divine knowledge encompasses both existent and non-existent things, and includes all categories: necessary, contingent, impossible, universal, and particular.

Checkpoint

A) It does not know them because they are not real.
B) It knows them as they are, including their non-existence.
C) It only knows them after they come into existence.
D) It knows them as possible, not as non-existent.

Key Takeaway
Divine knowledge is an eternal, all-encompassing attribute that discloses every thing—including non-existents—with absolute certainty, admitting no opposite.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that every single thing you do—every thought, every hidden deed—is fully disclosed to Allah in a way that admits no error? How does that reality affect your actions when you are alone? What is one habit you need to change because of this certainty?
?
If Allah’s knowledge encompasses even the impossible and non-existent, what excuse do you have for hiding your sins? Are you not aware that He knows them as they truly are? Write down one sin you have been concealing and commit to seeking His forgiveness today.
?
The definition describes divine knowledge as an eternal disclosure without any opposite. How does this attribute of Allah bring you both comfort and urgency? Reflect on a situation where you doubt Allah's knowledge of your struggle. Silently affirm: 'Allah knows it completely.' How does that change your attitude right now?

The Divine Attributes of Life, Power, and Will
The author defines three divine attributes: life, power, and will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions and functions of the divine attributes of life, power, and will as eternal attributes of Allah.

Core Idea
Divine life, power, and will are eternal attributes that enable knowledge, creation, and specification.

In this passage, the author continues enumerating the divine attributes by defining three essential ones: life, power, and will. Each is described as an eternal (azali) attribute with a specific function. Understanding these definitions is crucial for distinguishing between Allah's attributes and created qualities.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of divine life
Focus on how life is described as an eternal attribute that necessitates the soundness of knowledge and will. Note that life here is a metaphysical basis, not biological.
2
Read the definition of divine power
See how power is defined as an eternal attribute that makes possible the origination and annihilation of contingent things. This distinguishes Allah's power from created power.
3
Read the definition of divine will
Understand will as an eternal attribute that particularizes contingent things with one of their possible states, like existence or non-existence. This is the principle of specification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالثَّانِيَةُ: الْحَيَاةُ، كَمَا قَالَ (حَيَاتُهُ تَعَالَى) وَهِيَ صِفَةٌ أَزَلِيَّةٌ تُوجِبُ صِحَّةَ الْعِلْمِ وَالْإِرَادَةِ. وَالثَّالِثَةُ: الْقُدْرَةُ، كَمَا قَالَ: (وَقُدْرَةٌ)، وَهِيَ صِفَةٌ أَزَلِيَّةٌ يُتَأَتَّى بِهَا إِيجَادُ الْمُمْكِنِ وَإِعْدَامُهُ. وَالرَّابِعَةُ: (إِرَادَةٌ) وَهِيَ صِفَةٌ أَزَلِيَّةٌ تُخَصِّصُ الْمُمْكِنَ بِبَعْضِ مَا يَجُوزُ عَلَيْهِ مِنْ وُجُودٍ أَوْ عَدَمٍ، وَنَحْوِ ذَلِكَ.
And the second [attribute]: Life, as he said (His life Most High) and it is an eternal attribute that necessitates the soundness of knowledge and will. And the third: Power, as he said: (and power) and it is an eternal attribute by which the bringing into existence and annihilating of the contingent is made possible. And the fourth: Will, and it is an eternal attribute that particularizes the contingent with some of what is possible for it, such as existence or non-existence, and similar to that.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition of three divine attributes: life (al-ḥayāt), power (al-qudra), and will (al-irāda). The author follows the matn's enumeration. First, life is defined as an eternal attribute (ṣifat azaliyya) that is the basis for the soundness (ṣiḥḥa) of knowledge and will—meaning that knowledge and will cannot be attributed to one who is not living. Second, power is defined as an eternal attribute by which the bringing into existence (ījād) and annihilating (iʿdām) of contingent things (al-mumkin) is made possible; this distinguishes divine power from created power, which cannot originate existence. Third, will is defined as an eternal attribute that particularizes (tukhaṣṣiṣ) a contingent thing with one of its possible states, such as existence versus non-existence; it is the principle of specification. Each definition emphasizes eternality (azaliyya) and a distinct function.

Key Terms
الحياة
القدرة
الإرادة
صفة أزلية
تخصيص الممكن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That life is merely a biological property.
Correction: Here, life is a metaphysical attribute that enables knowledge and will, not a biological one.

Checkpoint
What is the function of the divine attribute of will?
A) To create things.
B) To know things.
C) To particularize a contingent with one of its possible states.
D) To bring life to things.

Key Takeaway
The divine attributes of life, power, and will are eternal and each serves a distinct function: life enables knowledge, power enables creation, and will enables specification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that feels unbearable, do you truly internalize that Allah's power alone brings it into existence and can remove it? How would your anxiety change if you lived by this belief every moment?
?
Your life right now—your health, wealth, relationships—is a result of Allah's will particularizing each detail. Are you content with His specification, or do you secretly wish for a different decree? This very moment, can you say with your heart, 'I am pleased with Allah as my Lord'?
?
If Allah's life is eternal and His will is decisive, then your knowledge and desires are entirely dependent on Him. Have you ever prayed to Allah to correct your will to align with His? What is one specific desire you need to surrender to His will today?

God Wills All That Happens
Sanusi asserts the universality of divine will using examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's will encompasses all existing things, including those that oppose His commands.

Core Idea
God wills everything that happens, even what He forbids.

This lesson tackles a subtle point about divine will: does God will only what He commands, or does He also will what He prohibits? The text clarifies that nothing escapes God's will, a cornerstone of Sunni theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key terms: 'irāda, kāʾin, mulk.' The author uses examples of Abu Bakr and Abu Jahl.
2
Identify the scope of divine will
Contrast what God commands (faith) with what He wills (both faith and disbelief). The passage says there is no difference.
3
Reflect on the implications
How does this affect our understanding of God's justice and power? The commentary explains that will is broader than command.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ ذَكَرَ النَّاظِمُ مَسْأَلَةً تَتَعَلَّقُ بِالْإِرَادَةِ، وَهِيَ أَنَّ مَا مِنْ شَيْءٍ يُوجَدُ إِلَّا وَاللَّهُ الْعَظِيمُ قَدْ أَرَادَهُ، فَقَالَ: (وَكُلُّ شَيْءٍ كَائِنٌ) أَيْ: مَوْجُودٌ (أَرَادَهُ) اللَّهُ عَزَّوَجَلَّ، أَيْ: أَرَادَ وُجُودَهُ، فَلَا يَقَعُ فِي مُلْكِهِ إِلَّا مَا يُرِيدُ. وَلَا فَرْقَ فِي ذَلِكَ بَيْنَ مَا أَمَرَ اللَّهُ بِهِ، كَإِيمَانِ أَبِي بَكْرٍ، وَإِيمَانِ سَائِرِ الْمُؤْمِنِينَ، بَلْ (وَإِنْ يَكُنْ بِضِدِّهِ) أَيْ بِضِدِّ ذَلِكَ الْكَائِنِ (قَدْ أَمَرَا) تَبَارَكَ وَتَعَالَى، كَكُفْرِ أَبِي جَهْلٍ، وَكُفْرِ بَقِيَّةِ الْكَافِرِينَ، فَإِنَّ اللَّهَ تَعَالَى أَرَادَهُ وَقَدْ أَمَرَ بِضِدِّهِ، وَهُوَ الْإِيمَانُ، فَالْكُلُّ مُرَادُ اللَّهِ تَعَالَى.
Then the poet mentioned a matter related to will, which is that there is nothing that exists except that Allah the Magnificent has willed it. So he said: 'And everything that occurs' i.e. exists, 'He willed it' meaning Allah Almighty willed its existence. So nothing occurs in His dominion except what He wills. And there is no difference in that between what Allah commanded, like the faith of Abu Bakr and the faith of all believers, rather even if it is the opposite of that thing that He commanded, like the disbelief of Abu Jahl and the disbelief of the rest of the disbelievers. For Allah Almighty willed it and He commanded its opposite, which is faith. So everything is willed by Allah Almighty.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a proof and application of the universality of divine will. The author begins by stating that the poet (al-nāẓim) raises an issue related to will (masʾala tataʿallaqu bi-l-irāda): there is nothing that exists except that God has willed it. This is a direct gloss of the matn line 'wa kullu shayʾin kāʾin arādahu'. The commentary expands: God wills the existence of every occurring thing; nothing occurs in His dominion except what He wills (fa-lā yaqaʿu fī mulkihi illā mā yurīd). The author then addresses a potential objection: does this include things He commanded, and even things He prohibited? He explains that there is no difference: God wills the faith of believers (which He also commands) and He wills the disbelief of disbelievers (which He forbids). The key point is that divine will is broader than divine command; everything that happens, whether compliant or contrary to command, is willed by God. So the lesson affirms that God's will is all-encompassing.

Key Terms
الإرادة
كل شيء كائن
مُلك الله
مراد الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God only wills what He commands; He does not will sinful actions.
Correction: The passage shows that God wills disbelief of Abu Jahl even though He commands faith, so His will extends to all occurrences, not just what He commands.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relationship between God's will and the disbelief of Abu Jahl?
A) God did not will it but permitted it.
B) God willed it even though He commanded its opposite.
C) God willed it and commanded it.
D) God did not will it because it is evil.

Key Takeaway
Everything that occurs, whether obedience or sin, is willed by God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God wills every sin you have ever committed, are you still fully responsible? How does this truth affect the way you confess your sins and seek forgiveness?
?
Think of a person you know who rejects Islam. According to this lesson, God wills their disbelief even as He commands them to believe. How should this understanding shape your attitude toward them—your prayers, your words, your actions?
?
When you face a painful trial or see injustice, do you truly believe that God willed it? If you do, how should that change your reaction—your anger, your despair, your trust in God’s wisdom?

Distinction Between Divine Will and Command
The author distinguishes divine will from command and refutes Mu'tazilite error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the critical distinction between Allah's will and His command, and why the Mu'tazilite position that God does not will evil is refuted by the principle 'what He willed was'.

Core Idea
Allah's will and command are distinct; what He wills always occurs, even if He does not command it.

This lesson confronts a deep theological puzzle: does God will everything that happens, including evil? The author, building on earlier foundations, first clarifies that divine will and divine command are not identical. He then refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that some things occur in God's dominion without His will. The passage provides a simple yet powerful proof from the well-known principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key claim: there is no necessary connection (lā talāzum) between will and command.
The passage uses technical terms: الإرادة (will) and الأمر (command).
2
Understand the distinction
Identify how the author defines will (an attribute that particularizes contingent things) and command (pertains to divine speech). Recognize their distinct natures.
3
Grasp the refutation of the Mu'tazila
The Mu'tazila claim that things may occur in God's dominion that He does not will, based on equating will with command. The author refutes this using the proof: 'what He willed was, and what He did not will was not' (مَا شَاءَ كَانَ). This establishes Allah's absolute sovereignty over all events.
This principle is a foundational axiom in Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَسَتَعْلَمُ أَنَّهُ لَا تَلَازُمَ بَيْنَ الْإِرَادَةِ وَالْأَمْرِ، كَمَا قَالَ: (فَالْقَصْدُ) وَالْإِرَادَةُ (غَيْرُ الْأَمْرِ بِالشَّيْءِ، بَلْ وَلَا يَسْتَلْزِمُهُ، لِأَنَّ الْإِرَادَةَ صِفَةٌ تُخَصِّصُ الْمُمْكِنَ بِبَعْضِ مَا يَجُوزُ عَلَيْهِ، كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ لَكَ، وَالْأَمْرُ يَرْجِعُ لِكَلَامِهِ عَزَّوَجَلَّ. (فَاطْرَحَ الْمِرَا) وَالنِّزَاعَ، يُشِيرُ إِلَى قَوْلِ الْمُعْتَزِلَةِ: «إِنَّهُ قَدْ يَقَعُ فِي مُلْكِهِ مَا لَا يُرِيدُ» بِنَاءً عَلَى اتِّحَادِ الْإِرَادَةِ وَالْأَمْرِ، وَهُوَ غَيْرُ صَحِيحٍ بِدَلِيلِ مَا شَاءَ كَانَ وَمَا لَمْ يَشَأْ لَمْ يَكُنْ، وَهُوَ عَزَّوَجَلَّ إِذَا أَرَادَ الْقَبِيحَ، كَالْكُفْرِ، فَإِنَّمَا هُوَ خَلْقُهُ وَإِرَادَتُهُ، تَعَالَى اللَّهُ عَمَّا يَقُولُ الظَّالِمُونَ عُلُوًّا كَبِيرًا. (فَقَدْ عَلِمْتَ) مِنْ قَوْلِ النَّاظِمِ: أَقْسَامُ الْكَائِنَاتِ وَكُلُّ شَيْءٍ كَائِنٍ ... إِلَى آخِرِهِ، (أَرْبَعًا أَقْسَامًا فِي الْكَائِنَاتِ) مَنْطُوقًا الْإِرَادَةَ وَالْأَمْرَ مَفْهُومًا: الْأَوَّلُ: مَأْمُورٌ بِهِ وَمُرَادٌ، كَإِيمَانِ أَبِي بَكْرٍ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ عَنْهُ.
And you will know that there is no necessary connection between will and command, as he said: 'Intention' and will are 'other than the command of a thing, rather it does not entail it because will is an attribute that particularizes the contingent with some of what is possible for it, as preceded for you, while command pertains to His speech Exalted. So 'abandon contention'. He refers to the statement of the Mu'tazila: 'Indeed there may occur in His dominion what He does not will' – on the basis of the identity of will and command – and it is incorrect by the proof of 'what He willed was, and what He did not will was not', and He Almighty when He wills the ugly, like disbelief, it is but His creation and His will – exalted is Allah above what the wrongdoers say, a great exaltation. 'So you have known' from the poet's saying: 'the divisions of existing things and everything that exists...' etc., 'four divisions in existing things' – by explicit mention of will and command and by implication: the first: commanded and willed, like the faith of Abu Bakr, may Allah be pleased with him.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument against the Mu'tazilite position that God does not will evil. The author first asserts that there is no necessary connection (lā talāzum) between divine will (al-irāda) and divine command (al-amr). He explains that will is an attribute that particularizes a contingent thing with one of its possibilities, while command is a matter of divine speech (kalām). Thus they are not identical and do not entail each other. He then refutes the Mu'tazilite claim that something occurs in God's dominion that He does not will – a claim based on equating will and command. The author gives a simple proof: 'what He willed was, and what He did not will was not' (mā shāʾa kāna wa mā lam yashaʾ lam yakun). He concludes that if God wills something ugly like disbelief, it is still His creation and will, and God is exalted above any charge of injustice. Finally, the author begins to enumerate the fourfold division of things based on will and command, which is derived from the matn. Only the first category is given in this passage: (1) commanded and willed, like the faith of Abu Bakr. The other three are presumably continued in the next section.

Key Terms
الإرادة
الأمر
ما شاء كان
المعتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's will and His command are identical, and therefore God does not will evil occurrences.
Correction: The passage clarifies that will and command are distinct attributes; God's will encompasses all that occurs, including evil, while His command pertains to what He prescribes. The principle 'ما شاء كان' affirms that nothing occurs against His will.

Checkpoint
Why do the Mu'tazila mistakenly claim that some things occur against God's will?
A) Because they deny God's power.
B) Because they identify will with command.
C) Because they think God only commands good.
D) Because they believe in free will.

Key Takeaway
Allah's will encompasses all that occurs; His command is distinct and pertains to what He prescribes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own sins, can you sincerely accept that they occurred by Allah's will, and yet still hold yourself fully accountable for them? What does that do to your sense of reliance on His mercy?
?
If you truly believed that Allah wills everything that happens—including the suffering you have experienced—how would that change the way you deal with pain and injustice? Will you now consciously affirm His will in your trials?
?
The Mu'tazilite error softened the reality of Allah's absolute sovereignty. In what areas of your life do you subtly resist the idea that His will covers all—preferring to think some things are outside His control? Are you ready to submit those areas to His will today?

Divine Attributes

6 min
The Distinction Between Divine Will and Command
Mashat defines the distinction and refutes Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine will and command are distinct, that God's will encompasses all things including evil, while His command is specifically for good, and the Sunni proof against the Mu'tazila position.

Core Idea
God's will and command are not the same; He may will what He does not command.

In this passage, Shaykh Mashat clarifies a crucial distinction between two divine attributes: will (irada) and command (amr). The Mu'tazila mistakenly equated them, leading to theological errors about God's will and what occurs in His kingdom. By understanding this distinction, we safeguard the correct Sunni doctrine that God's will is universal, while His command is only for righteousness.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Let the author's own phrasing establish the distinction before you interpret.
2
Identify the definitions given
Notice how Mashat defines will as an attribute that specifies a possible thing among its possible states, and command as a matter of divine speech. This is the foundation of the distinction.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila error
The Mu'tazila claimed that what occurs in God's kingdom must be what He wills, based on equating will and command. Identify their claim and the proof that refutes it.
The proof is the well-known statement: 'Whatever He wills happens and whatever He does not will does not happen.'
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Mashat affirms that when God wills evil, like disbelief, it is His creation and His will. Consider how this upholds divine sovereignty without attributing injustice to God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالقصد والإرادة غير الأمر بالشيء، بل ولا يستلزمه، لأن الإرادة صفة تخصص الممكن ببعض ما يجوز عليه، كما تقدّم لك، والأمر يرجع لكلامه عز وجل. (فاطرح المرا) والنزاع، يشير إلى قول المعتزلة: «إنه قد يقع في ملكه ما لا يريد بناء على اتحاد الإرادة والأمر، وهو غير صحيح بدليل ما شاء كان وما لم يشأ لم يكن» (١)، وهو عز وجل إذا أراد القبيح، كالكفر، فإنما هو خلقه وإرادته، تعالى الله عما يقول الظالمون علوا كبيرًا.
For the intention and the will are not the command concerning a thing, nay, they do not entail it, because the will is an attribute that specifies the possible with some of what is possible for it, as was presented to you, and the command goes back to His speech, may He be exalted and glorified. (So abandon dispute) and contention, referring to the statement of the Mu'tazila: 'Indeed, what He does not will may occur in His kingdom based on the unification of will and command,' and it is not correct by the proof of 'whatever He wills happens and whatever He does not will does not happen' (1), and He, exalted, when He wills the evil, such as disbelief, it is only His creation and His will, exalted is God above what the wrongdoers say, a great exaltation.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage defines the distinction between divine will (irada) and command (amr). The author states that they are distinct and do not entail each other. He describes will as an attribute that specifies a possible thing among its possible states, while command is a matter of divine speech. He then refutes the Mu'tazila who claimed that what occurs in God's kingdom must be what He wills, based on equating will and command. He counters with the proof that whatever God wills happens and whatever He does not will does not happen, and that when God wills evil like disbelief, it is His creation and His will, and God is exalted above what they say.

Key Terms
إرادة (irada)
أمر (amr)
المعتزلة (Mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's will and command are identical, so He only wills what He commands.
Correction: They are distinct; God wills all things, including evil, but commands only good. Thus, everything that occurs is by His will, but not everything is commanded by Him.

Checkpoint
What proof does the author cite to refute the Mu'tazila's claim that only what God wills occurs?
A) A hadith: 'Whatever He wills happens and whatever He does not will does not happen'
B) Logical necessity based on divine unity
C) Consensus of the Companions
D) An ayat from the Quran

Key Takeaway
God's will is universal, encompassing both good and evil, while His command pertains only to what He approves.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you ever comfort yourself with the thought that 'God willed it', ignoring that He commands you not to sin? How does the distinction between will and command expose that self-deception?
?
The Mu'tazila denied that God wills evil because they thought it would make Him unjust. Are there areas in your life where you redefine God's attributes to avoid accepting a truth about Him that challenges your comfort?
?
God wills disbelief in some, yet commands faith to all. How does this truth force you to rely solely on His mercy for your own faith, rather than on your own effort or worthiness?

The Fifth Attribute: Speech (Kalam)
The author enumerates speech as the fifth attribute of meanings, citing the poem line.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that speech (kalam) is one of the seven eternal attributes of God according to Ash'ari theology, and that it is an attribute of meaning, not created.

Core Idea
God's speech is an eternal attribute, not a created thing.

After discussing power, will, knowledge, life, hearing, and sight, the author now turns to the seventh attribute: speech. The poem line links speech with hearing and sight, emphasizing that God is the Agent and Chooser. This lesson establishes that divine speech is an eternal attribute of His essence.

Guided Path

3

The poem line is a summary of the attributes; the commentary clarifies the enumeration.

Divine Speech as an Eternal Psychical Attribute
Definition of divine speech as an eternal psychical attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of divine speech as an eternal psychical attribute, distinct from temporal or physical speech.

Core Idea
God's speech is eternal and unlike human speech — it is not composed of letters or sounds.

In this lesson, we examine the fifth divine attribute: speech (kalam). The passage clarifies that divine speech is not a physical phenomenon but an eternal, psychical attribute subsisting in God's essence. Understanding this distinction is crucial to avoiding anthropomorphic misconceptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without overthinking. Then read the literal translation to get the author's intended meaning.
Notice how the passage immediately defines kalam as a 'psychical attribute'.
2
Identify the key characteristics
List the characteristics mentioned: eternal, psychical, not letter/sound, no precedence/delay, no beginning/end, indicates all known things.
These negations distinguish divine speech from human speech.
3
Reflect on the unifying concept
Ask yourself: why is it important that divine speech is not made of letters or sounds? How does this affect our understanding of the Qur'an?
The last verse (42:11) reinforces that nothing is like Him.

Source Text

والخامس من صفات المعاني الكلام، كما قال: (كلامه) تعالى القائم بذاته، وهو صفة أزلية نفسية، ليست بحرف ولا صوت، ولا يوصف بتقديم ولا تأخير ولا بداية ولا نهاية، وهو دال على جميع المعلومات، وَلَيْسَ كَمِثْلِهِ شَيْءٌ وَهُوَ السَّمِيعُ الْبَصِيرُ.

Source pages p. 63

Commentary
This passage defines the fifth attribute among the attributes of ma'ani: speech (kalam). It states that it is an eternal (azali) psychical (nafsi) attribute, not composed of letters or sounds, and not subject to temporal qualifications like precedence or delay, nor to beginning or end. It encompasses all known things, and God is described as Hearing and Seeing. The definition emphasizes that divine speech is not akin to human speech.

Hearing and Sight: Divine Attributes of Disclosure
Definition of hearing and sight as eternal attributes of disclosure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that hearing and sight are two eternal divine attributes that provide a unique and real disclosure of all existents, distinct from each other and from the attribute of knowledge.

Core Idea
Hearing and sight are real, eternal attributes of Allah, each providing a unique disclosure distinct from knowledge.

In this passage, the author introduces the sixth and seventh attributes: hearing and sight. He emphasizes that these are not merely metaphors but real, eternal attributes that fully disclose all existents in a way that differs in reality from disclosure through knowledge. Understanding this distinction is crucial for a correct conception of Allah's attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Notice how the author presents hearing and sight as the sixth and seventh attributes.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ينكشف بهما جميع الموجودات انكشافا تاما' – 'by which all existents are fully disclosed.'
2
Understand the definition
The passage defines hearing and sight as eternal attributes. Reflect on what it means for an attribute to be eternal and 'fully disclosing' all existents.
Contrast this with the temporary or partial disclosure we experience.
3
Identify the key distinction
The passage states that disclosure through hearing and sight differs 'in reality' from disclosure through knowledge, and that each of the two differs from the other. Ask yourself why this distinction matters.
This guards against reducing hearing and sight to mere knowledge or conflating them.
4
Reflect on the threefold difference
Note that the passage affirms three distinct modes of divine disclosure: through knowledge, through hearing, and through sight. Consider how each contributes to Allah's complete awareness.
This understanding supports the doctrine that each attribute has a unique reality.

The passage further notes that the disclosure through hearing and sight is fundamentally different in reality from disclosure through knowledge, and similarly, disclosure through one differs from the other. This emphasizes the real and distinct nature of each attribute.

The Two Meanings of Divine Speech: Sensory and Psychical
Explanation of the homonymous use of 'kalam Allah' for sensory and psychical speech.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between sensory and psychical divine speech, and why the eternal attribute of speech is indivisible and uncreated.

Core Idea
Divine speech has two meanings: sensory (letters/sounds) and psychical (eternal attribute).

The term 'kalam Allah' is often misunderstood as univocal. In this passage, the commentator clarifies that it is used homonymously for two distinct realities: the eternal psychical speech that is an attribute of God, and the sensory speech that consists of letters and sounds. Understanding this distinction is crucial for avoiding theological errors about God's speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow. Pay attention to the definitions of sensory and psychical speech.
2
Identify the two meanings
Locate where the passage defines 'sensory speech' (kalam hissi) and 'psychical speech' (kalam nafsi). Note their characteristics.
Sensory speech involves letters/sounds; psychical speech is an eternal attribute.
3
Understand the homonymy
The passage states that 'kalam Allah' is used for both meanings. Ask yourself why the same term can refer to two different things without ambiguity in context.
This is called ishtirak (homonymy) and is resolved by convention (urf).
4
Explore the implications
Reflect on why psychical speech cannot be divided or described with time-related attributes. How does this affect the understanding of divine speech in scriptures and prophethood?
The passage connects this to the division of speech into command, prohibition, etc., which applies only to sensory speech.
5
Address the misconception
Consider the common misunderstanding that God's speech to Moses was a created sound. The passage clarifies it was psychical speech according to the majority of Ash'aris and some Maturidis.
This guards against anthropomorphism and createdness of the divine attribute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن كلام الله يطلق بالاشتراك على الحسي والنفسي الذي هو الصفة القديمة، فهو حقيقة عرفية في كل، فالحسي: ما كان بحرف وصوت ومدلوله بعض مدلول الكلام النفسي القديم القائم بذاته تعالى والنفسي: ما ليس بحرف ولا صوت، ولا يوصف بتقديم ولا تأخير، ولا تقسيم ولا بداية ولا نهاية، وهو قديم ليس بمخلوق. فالكتب السماوية دالة على بعض مدلول الكلام النفسي، ولا يحيط بكل مدلوله إلا هو، لأن مدلول الكلام النفسي الواجبات، والمستحيلات، والجائزات تفصيلا، وأما الكتب السماوية فقد دلت على بعض الواجبات تفصيلا، وكل الواجبات إجمالاً، وكذا المستحيلات والجائزات، وتكليم الله لموسى على الجبل كان بالكلام النفسي على التحقيق عند الأشاعرة وبعض الماتريدية، خلافًا للمعتزلة والبعض الآخر من الماتريدية، فتقسيم الكلام إلى أمر، ونهي، وخبر واستخبار، ووعد ووعيد، إنما هو لتلك المدلولات التي دل عليها الكلام الحسي، وأما الصفة القديمة فيستحيل انقسامها.
Know that 'the speech of God' is used homonymously for the sensory and the psychical, which is the eternal attribute. So it is a conventional reality in each. The sensory is what is by letter and sound, and its denotation is part of the denotation of the eternal psychical speech, which is subsistent in His essence. The psychical is what is not by letter or sound, nor described with precedence or delay, nor division, nor beginning nor end, and it is eternal, not created. Thus the heavenly books indicate some of the denotation of the psychical speech, and none encompasses all its denotation except Him, because the denotation of the psychical speech is the necessary, impossible, and contingent things in detail. As for the heavenly books, they indicate some of the necessary things in detail, and all the necessary things in summary, and likewise the impossible and contingent things. And God's speaking to Moses on the mountain was with psychical speech according to the verified view of the Ash'aris and some Maturidis, contrary to the Mu'tazila and some other Maturidis. Thus the division of speech into command, prohibition, news, inquiry, promise, and threat is only for those denotations that sensory speech indicates, whereas the eternal attribute is impossible to divide.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a detailed clarification of the term 'kalam Allah' (divine speech). It states that the term is used homonymously (bi'l-ishtirak) for sensory (hissi) and psychical (nafsi) speech. The psychical speech is the eternal attribute itself, not composed of letters or sounds, and it is not subject to division, precedence, or delay. The sensory speech consists of letters and sounds and denotes part of the meanings of the psychical speech. The scriptures (heavenly books) indicate some of those meanings in detail, but only God encompasses all. The psychical speech includes all necessary, impossible, and possible things in detail. The passage also discusses the communication (taklim) to Moses on the mountain, which, according to Ash'aris and some Maturidis, was through psychical speech. Finally, the division of speech into command, prohibition, news, etc., applies to the sensory speech's denotations, not to the eternal attribute itself, which is indivisible.

Key Terms
كلام حسي
كلام نفسي
اشتراك
صفة قديمة
تكليم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's speech to Moses was a created voice or sensory speech.
Correction: According to the passage, God's speaking to Moses on the mountain was with psychical speech, as held by Ash'aris and some Maturidis.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct view regarding God's speech to Moses?
A) It was through sensory speech.
B) It was through psychical speech according to Ash'aris and some Maturidis.
C) It was a created voice.
D) It was not real.

Key Takeaway
God's speech is primarily an eternal psychical attribute, and sensory speech is a created expression of some of its meanings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you treat it as merely a human expression of divine meaning, or as the very speech of God? How does the distinction between sensory and psychical speech affect your reverence for the words you utter? Commit to a specific change in how you approach recitation from now on.
?
The passage says the eternal attribute is indivisible and encompasses all necessary, impossible, and contingent things in detail. How does this affect your understanding of God's knowledge? Is your view of God limited to what you can derive from scripture, or do you acknowledge that His speech infinitely surpasses human comprehension? What will you do to deepen your awe of His attribute?
?
God's speech to Moses was psychical, not sensory. Does this make the encounter less ‘real’ in your mind? Reflect on whether you have unconsciously limited God to human categories. How will you cultivate a conviction that God's attributes are unique and not analogous to creation?

Divine Attributes: Speech, Hearing, and Sight
Defines speech, hearing, and sight as eternal divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that speech, hearing, and sight are eternal attributes of Allah, distinct from each other and from knowledge.

Core Idea
Allah's speech, hearing, and sight are coeternal, non-sensory attributes that perfectly disclose all existence.

This lesson examines the divine attributes of speech, hearing, and sight as presented by the commentator. These are among the essential attributes of Allah, and the passage clarifies their eternal nature and their functional distinction from one another and from divine knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage aloud
The passage defines divine speech as an eternal self-attribute, then introduces hearing and sight as a pair. Read it slowly to absorb the rhythm.
2
Identify the attributes discussed
Note that speech is treated first, then hearing and sight together. The commentator stresses their eternity and their function: disclosure of all existents.
3
Understand the uniqueness of each attribute
The passage explicitly states that disclosure through hearing/sight differs from disclosure through knowledge, and each differs from the other. This affirms that attributes are distinct realities.
4
Reflect on the implications
Why does it matter that these attributes are eternal and not created? How does this distinguish Islamic theology from views that reduce God's attributes to His essence?
This connects to the broader theme of tawhid and divine perfection.

واعلم أن الانكشاف بالسمع والبصر يغاير في الحقيقة الانكشاف بالعلم، كما أن الانكشاف بإحداهما يغاير الانكشاف] بالأخرى.
(١) سورة الشورى، الآية (١١)، اعلم أن كلام الله يطلق بالاشتراك على الحسي والنفسي الذي هو الصفة القديمة، فهو حقيقة عرفية في كل، فالحسي: ما كان بحرف وصوت ومدلوله بعض مدلول الكلام النفسي القديم القائم بذاته تعالى والنفسي: ما ليس بحرف ولا صوت، ولا يوصف بتقديم ولا تأخير، ولا تقسيم ولا بداية ولا نهاية، وهو قديم ليس بمخلوق
فالكتب السماوية دالة على بعض مدلول الكلام النفسي، ولا يحيط بكل مدلوله إلا هو، لأن مدلول الكلام النفسي الواجبات، والمستحيلات، والجائزات تفصيلا، وأما الكتب السماوية فقد دلت على بعض الواجبات تفصيلا، وكل الواجبات إجمالاً، وكذا المستحيلات والجائزات، وتكليم الله لموسى على الجبل كان بالكلام النفسي على التحقيق عند الأشاعرة وبعض الماتريدية، خلافًا للمعتزلة والبعض الآخر من الماتريدية، فتقسيم الكلام إلى أمر، ونهي، وخبر واستخبار، ووعد ووعيد، إنما هو لتلك المدلولات التي دل عليها الكلام الحسي، وأما الصفة القديمة فيستحيل انقسامها.
His speech and hearing and sight – so He is the God, the Agent, the Chooser 36. Explanation of Shaykh Hasan Muhammad al-Mashshāṭ al-Malikī al-Mālikī: And it is self-subsisting, and it is an eternal attribute of the self, not a letter nor a sound, and it is not described with precedence nor delay nor beginning nor end, and it indicates all information. And there is nothing like Him, and He is the Hearing, the Seeing (Sūra al-Shūrā, verse 11). And the sixth and seventh are hearing and sight, as he said: (and hearing and sight) with a kasra on the hamza, a verbal noun from abṣara, and what is meant is sight. So hearing and sight are two eternal attributes by which all existents are disclosed with complete disclosure. And know that disclosure through hearing and sight differs in reality from disclosure through knowledge, just as disclosure through one of them differs from disclosure through the other. (1) Sūra al-Shūrā, verse (11). Know that the speech of God is applied by common usage to both the sensory and the self, which is the eternal attribute, so it is a conventional reality in each. So the sensory is what is by letter and sound and its denotation is some of the denotation of the eternal self-speech subsisting in His essence, the Exalted. And the self-speech is what is not by letter nor sound, and is not described with precedence nor delay, nor division nor beginning nor end, and it is eternal, not created. So the heavenly books indicate some of the denotation of self-speech, and no one encompasses all its denotation except Him, because the denotation of self-speech is the obligatory, the impossible, and the possible in detail. And as for the heavenly books, they have indicated some of the obligatory in detail, and all the obligatory in summary, and similarly the impossible and the possible. And God's speaking to Musa on the mountain was by self-speech according to the investigation of the Ash'aris and some of the Māturīdīs, contrary to the Mu'tazila and the other part of the Māturīdīs. So the division of speech into command, prohibition, news, interrogation, promise, and threat is only for those denotations which the sensory speech indicates, and as for the eternal attribute, its division is impossible.
Source pages p. 64

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the line of poetry 'His speech and hearing and sight – so He is the God, the Agent, the Chooser.' The commentator begins by explaining the attribute of divine speech (kalam). He states that it is an eternal attribute of the self (nafsī), not consisting of letters or sounds, and not subject to temporal qualities like precedence or delay. It indicates all information, i.e., all objects of knowledge. Then he mentions the sixth and seventh attributes: hearing (sam') and sight (baṣar). These are also eternal attributes through which all existents are fully disclosed. The commentator emphasizes that the disclosure through hearing and sight is different in reality from disclosure through knowledge, and each of hearing and sight discloses in a way distinct from the other. He then includes a long footnote on divine speech. In the footnote, he explains that the term 'speech of God' is used conventionally (ḥaqīqa ʿurfiyya) for two types: sensory speech (al-ḥissī) and self-speech (al-nafsī). Sensory speech consists of letters and sounds and indicates only part of what self-speech indicates. Self-speech is the eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence, not composed of letters or sounds. The heavenly books (Torah, Gospel, Qur'an) indicate some of the denotation of self-speech in detail and all of it in summary. Only God encompasses all its denotation, which includes all obligatory, impossible, and possible things in detail. The commentator notes that, according to the Ash'aris and some Māturīdīs, God's speaking to Moses on the mountain was by self-speech, contrary to the Mu'tazila and some other Māturīdīs. Finally, he clarifies that the division of speech into command, prohibition, etc., applies to sensory speech; the eternal attribute itself is indivisible.

Key Terms
كلام (speech)
سمع (hearing)
بصر (sight)
الصفة القديمة (eternal attribute)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, how are hearing and sight related to disclosure?
A) They are identical to knowledge.
B) They disclose all existents completely.
C) They are created in time.
D) They are limited to certain objects.

Key Takeaway
Allah's speech, hearing, and sight are distinct, eternal, non-sensory attributes that perfectly manifest all things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
How does the truth that Allah's speech is not a letter or sound change the way you approach the Qur'an? Will you now listen to it as His eternal, living word, or still treat it as a mere book?
?
If Allah's hearing and sight disclose every existent completely—including your secret thoughts and actions—what sin have you been committing as if He does not see? When will you repent?
?
Do you catch yourself imagining Allah's attributes as similar to human ones? What specific mental image do you need to destroy, and what verse from this lesson (e.g., 'There is nothing like Him') will you repeat until your heart submits?

The Nature of Divine Speech: Sensory and Self-Speech
Explains the two types of divine speech and their relationship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between sensory speech (letters and sounds) and self-speech (the eternal attribute) and how they relate to revelation.

Core Idea
God's speech is twofold: eternal self-speech and temporal sensory speech; the latter points to the former.

This lesson explores the twofold meaning of 'God's speech' in Islamic theology. The commentator clarifies that the term can refer either to the eternal attribute subsisting in God's essence (self-speech) or to the revealed scriptures composed of letters and sounds (sensory speech). Understanding this distinction is crucial to avoid anthropomorphism and to appreciate the nature of divine revelation.

Guided Path
1
Read the footnote carefully
The footnote begins by stating that 'speech of God' is applied conventionally to both sensory and self-speech. Read it through to grasp the two categories.
2
Distinguish between sensory and self-speech
Sensory speech is made of letters and sounds; it indicates only part of what self-speech indicates. Self-speech is the eternal attribute, not composed of letters/sounds, and it encompasses all that is obligatory, impossible, and possible.
3
Consider the example of Moses
The passage mentions that God's speech to Moses on the mountain was self-speech according to Ash'aris and some Māturīdīs. This illustrates the theological view that prophetic revelation is not God literally speaking in sounds.
4
Understand the division of speech
The division into command, prohibition, etc., applies only to sensory speech; the eternal attribute itself is indivisible.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila and some Māturīdīs believed that God's speech to Moses was a created sound.
Correction: The Ash'aris and other Māturīdīs affirm it was the eternal self-speech, not a temporal sound.

D) Because it is created.

Key Takeaway
Divine speech primarily refers to the eternal attribute of self-speech, while scripture is its temporal, sensory expression.

?

?

?
If self-speech is indivisible, then God's command and His news are not separate realities in Him. How does this unity of divine speech affect the seriousness with which you treat every command and promise in the Quran?

The Free Agent and the Attributes of Meanings
The commentator derives four attributes of meanings from 'al-Fāʿil al-Mukhtār' and provides evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of God as the Free Agent (al-Fāʿil al-Mukhtār) and the four attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī) that are derived from it, along with the Quranic and hadith evidence for this belief.

Core Idea
God acts by free choice, not by nature or cause, and this belief is obligatory.

This lesson unpacks a key phrase from the poem: 'al-Fāʿil al-Mukhtār'. The commentator explains that this phrase yields four essential attributes of meanings: knowledge, life, power, and will. He also cites Quranic verses and a hadith to support the belief that God is the sole free agent, and that submission to His commands is obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the Free Agent
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning up to the mention of the hadith. Notice how the commentator derives the four attributes from the phrase 'al-Fāʿil al-Mukhtār'.
The attributes are knowledge, life, power, and will.
2
Identify the evidence
The commentator quotes Quran 28:68 and a hadith about the taste of faith. Reflect on how these support the doctrine of God as the Free Agent.
3
Understand the obligation
The passage emphasizes that this belief is obligatory for every accountable person. Consider why this is central to Islamic theology.
Belief in God's free agency is tied to tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم فرّع على بعض صفات المعاني، وهي العلم والحياة، والقدرة، والإرادة بقوله: (فهو الإله الواحد المعبود بحق وهو الفاعل المختار الذي إن شاء فعل، وإن شاء ترك، لا فاعل بالطبع ولا بالعلة، وهذا الاعتقاد هو الواجب على المكلف، وَرَبُّكَ يَخْلُقُ مَا يَشَآءُ وَيَخْتَارُ مَا كَانَ لَهُمُ الْخِيَرَةُ سُبْحَانَ اللهِ وَتَعَالَى عَمَّا يُشْرِكُونَ (١ ) . كما يجب على المكلّف أن ينقاد لأوامر الله تعالى، واجتناب نواهيه فقد قال صلى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ: «ذاق طعم الإيمان من رضي بالله ربا وبالإسلام دينا وبمحمد صلى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ نبيّا » (۲) ، إلى غير ذلك من الآيات، والأحاديث الدالة على ترك التدبير وتفويض الأمر إلى الخبير العليم. قال أهل المعرفة: «من لا يُدَبُر دُبر له»، وانظر «التنوير في إسقاط التدبير (۳) لسيدي ابن عطاء الله قُدّس سرّه.
واعلم أن هذه الصفات السبع هي المتفق عليها، ولم يذكر الناظم الصفات المعنوية الملازمة للمعاني، وهو كونه تعالى عالمًا... إلى آخره، لأن الحق كما قاله الشارح (٤): «ما ذهب إليه إمامنا، إمام أهل السنة والجماعة، أبو الحسن رَضَ اللَّهُ عَنْهُ من أنها ليست زائدة على المعاني
Then he derived some attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī), namely knowledge, life, power, and will, by his saying: (So He is the God, the One, the truly worshiped, and He is the Free Agent who if He wills does, and if He wills leaves; not an agent by nature nor by cause. And this belief is obligatory upon the accountable one. And your Lord creates what He wills and chooses; they have no choice. Glory be to God and exalted above what they associate (Sūra al-Qaṣaṣ, verse 68). And just as it is obligatory upon the accountable one to submit to the commands of God Almighty and avoid His prohibitions, for he, peace and blessings be upon him, said: 'He has tasted the flavor of faith who is pleased with God as Lord, with Islam as religion, and with Muhammad as prophet' (reported by Muslim). And other verses and hadiths indicate abandoning self-management and delegating the affair to the Expert, the Knowing. The people of knowledge said: 'Whoever does not manage, it is managed for him.' And refer to 'Al-Tanwīr fī Isqāṭ al-Tadbīr' by my master Ibn ʿAṭāʾ Allāh, may his secret be sanctified. And know that these seven attributes are the agreed-upon ones. And the author did not mention the meaning attributes (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya) that accompany the attributes of meanings, namely His being Knower, etc., because the truth, as the commentator said, is what our Imam, the Imam of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jamāʿa, Abu al-Ḥasan, may God be pleased with him, held: that they are not additional over the attributes of meanings.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the second half of the poem line: '... so He is the God, the Free Agent.' The commentator explains that the author (al-nāẓim) derived from this phrase the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī): knowledge, life, power, and will. He clarifies that God is the one true God, the Free Agent who acts by choice, not by nature or by cause. This belief is obligatory for every accountable person (mukallaf). The commentator supports this with Quranic verse (al-Qaṣaṣ 28:68) and a hadith about the taste of faith, and he emphasizes submission to God's commands and delegation of affairs to Him. Then he addresses why the author did not mention the meaning attributes (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya), which are the entailments of the attributes of meanings, such as 'Being Knower' (ʿālim). He states that the seven attributes (life, knowledge, power, will, hearing, sight, speech) are agreed upon. The omission is due to the position of Imam Abu al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī, the imam of Ahl al-Sunna, who held that the meaning attributes are not additional to the attributes of meanings. In other words, saying 'God is Knower' is not a distinct attribute beyond knowledge, but rather the very attribution of knowledge.

Key Terms
صفات المعاني (attributes of meanings)
الفاعل المختار (the free agent)
المكلف (accountable person)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following attributes are derived from God being al-Fāʿil al-Mukhtār?
A) Knowledge, Life, Power, Will
B) Knowledge, Life, Power, Speech
C) Hearing, Sight, Speech
D) Knowledge, Power, Will, Hearing

Key Takeaway
God's free agency is a fundamental doctrine; He acts by will, and belief in this is obligatory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you truly content with God's choices for your life, even when they clash with your own desires? If not, what does that say about your belief in His free agency? This week, consciously relinquish one major plan and say 'I am pleased with God's will.'
?
When you face a difficult decision, do you first submit to God's will or rely on your own planning? The quote says 'Whoever does not manage, it is managed for him.' Will you dare to let go of control in one area today?
?
The hadith mentions tasting the flavor of faith through being pleased with God as Lord. Do you experience that sweetness in your daily life? What specific complaint or dissatisfaction is robbing you of that taste right now?

Why the Author Omitted the Meaning Attributes
Explains why the author omitted the meaning attributes, citing the Ash'ari position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author omitted the meaning attributes (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya) and the Ash'ari position that they are not additional to the attributes of meanings.

Core Idea
For Ash'ari, 'God is Knower' is the very attribution of knowledge, not a separate attribute.

The poem lists seven attributes but leaves out the meaning attributes that accompany them. Why? The commentator reveals that this omission follows the view of Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, the imam of Ahl al-Sunna, who held that saying 'God is Knower' is not an additional attribute beyond knowledge itself. This lesson clarifies that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the omission
Read the second part of the passage starting from 'And know that these seven attributes...' to the end. Notice the mention of the agreed-upon seven attributes and the omission of the meaning attributes.
2
Understand the distinction
The commentator explains that the meaning attributes (e.g., being Knower) are not additional over the attributes of meanings (e.g., knowledge). Reflect on why this matters in Islamic theology.
This is a subtle but important theological point.
3
Know the Ash'ari position
Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari held that the meaning attributes are not separate existent attributes. Instead, they are the very description of God through the attributes of meanings.
This avoids multiplying attributes unnecessarily.
