ثم فرّع على بعض صفات المعاني، وهي العلم والحياة، والقدرة، والإرادة بقوله: (فهو الإله الواحد المعبود بحق وهو الفاعل المختار الذي إن شاء فعل، وإن شاء ترك، لا فاعل بالطبع ولا بالعلة، وهذا الاعتقاد هو الواجب على المكلف، وَرَبُّكَ يَخْلُقُ مَا يَشَآءُ وَيَخْتَارُ مَا كَانَ لَهُمُ الْخِيَرَةُ سُبْحَانَ اللهِ وَتَعَالَى عَمَّا يُشْرِكُونَ (١ ) . كما يجب على المكلّف أن ينقاد لأوامر الله تعالى، واجتناب نواهيه فقد قال صلى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ: «ذاق طعم الإيمان من رضي بالله ربا وبالإسلام دينا وبمحمد صلى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ نبيّا » (۲) ، إلى غير ذلك من الآيات، والأحاديث الدالة على ترك التدبير وتفويض الأمر إلى الخبير العليم. قال أهل المعرفة: «من لا يُدَبُر دُبر له»، وانظر «التنوير في إسقاط التدبير (۳) لسيدي ابن عطاء الله قُدّس سرّه.
واعلم أن هذه الصفات السبع هي المتفق عليها، ولم يذكر الناظم الصفات المعنوية الملازمة للمعاني، وهو كونه تعالى عالمًا... إلى آخره، لأن الحق كما قاله الشارح (٤): «ما ذهب إليه إمامنا، إمام أهل السنة والجماعة، أبو الحسن رَضَ اللَّهُ عَنْهُ من أنها ليست زائدة على المعاني
Then he derived some attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī), namely knowledge, life, power, and will, by his saying: (So He is the God, the One, the truly worshiped, and He is the Free Agent who if He wills does, and if He wills leaves; not an agent by nature nor by cause. And this belief is obligatory upon the accountable one. And your Lord creates what He wills and chooses; they have no choice. Glory be to God and exalted above what they associate (Sūra al-Qaṣaṣ, verse 68). And just as it is obligatory upon the accountable one to submit to the commands of God Almighty and avoid His prohibitions, for he, peace and blessings be upon him, said: 'He has tasted the flavor of faith who is pleased with God as Lord, with Islam as religion, and with Muhammad as prophet' (reported by Muslim). And other verses and hadiths indicate abandoning self-management and delegating the affair to the Expert, the Knowing. The people of knowledge said: 'Whoever does not manage, it is managed for him.' And refer to 'Al-Tanwīr fī Isqāṭ al-Tadbīr' by my master Ibn ʿAṭāʾ Allāh, may his secret be sanctified. And know that these seven attributes are the agreed-upon ones. And the author did not mention the meaning attributes (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya) that accompany the attributes of meanings, namely His being Knower, etc., because the truth, as the commentator said, is what our Imam, the Imam of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jamāʿa, Abu al-Ḥasan, may God be pleased with him, held: that they are not additional over the attributes of meanings.
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the second half of the poem line: '... so He is the God, the Free Agent.' The commentator explains that the author (al-nāẓim) derived from this phrase the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī): knowledge, life, power, and will. He clarifies that God is the one true God, the Free Agent who acts by choice, not by nature or by cause. This belief is obligatory for every accountable person (mukallaf). The commentator supports this with Quranic verse (al-Qaṣaṣ 28:68) and a hadith about the taste of faith, and he emphasizes submission to God's commands and delegation of affairs to Him. Then he addresses why the author did not mention the meaning attributes (al-ṣifāt al-maʿnawiyya), which are the entailments of the attributes of meanings, such as 'Being Knower' (ʿālim). He states that the seven attributes (life, knowledge, power, will, hearing, sight, speech) are agreed upon. The omission is due to the position of Imam Abu al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī, the imam of Ahl al-Sunna, who held that the meaning attributes are not additional to the attributes of meanings. In other words, saying 'God is Knower' is not a distinct attribute beyond knowledge, but rather the very attribution of knowledge.

Key Terms
الصفات المعنوية (meaning attributes)
صفات المعاني (attributes of meanings)
أبو الحسن الأشعري (Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the meaning attributes (e.g., being Knower) are separate, positive attributes beyond the attributes of meanings.
Correction: According to the Ash'ari school, meaning attributes are not additional entities; they are the very attribution of the corresponding attribute of meaning.

Checkpoint
Why did the author omit the meaning attributes from his poem?
A) He forgot to mention them.
B) He considered them unnecessary for the poem.
C) He followed the view of Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari that they are not additional to the attributes of meanings.
D) He was a Mu'tazili.

Key Takeaway
The Ash'ari position holds that meaning attributes like 'Knower' are the very attribution of the corresponding attributes of meanings, not separate realities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'God is All-Knowing,' do you ever catch yourself imagining a separate 'knowingness' floating alongside His knowledge? How does this lesson call you to purify your mental image of Allah?
?
How might the Ash'ari emphasis on not multiplying attributes beyond necessity protect you from inadvertently adding complexities in your relationship with Allah? Are there areas where you layer unnecessary concepts onto your faith?
?
If the fact that God is 'Knower' is not an extra attribute but simply the attribution of His knowledge, how does that reshape the way you approach His names in your duʿā' and worship?

Why the Poet Omitted the Attribute States
Explains omission of attribute states and states Ash'ari position.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the poet of al-Kharida omitted the attribute states and what the correct Ash'ari position is regarding their ontological status.

Core Idea
Attribute states are not separate entities; they are the subsistence of attributes in the essence.

This passage explains a subtle but important point in Ash'ari theology: the poet omitted the attribute states (الصفات المعنوية) from his poem. The commentator clarifies that these states are not additional to the attributes of meaning, but are merely expressions of those attributes subsisting in the divine essence. This position, attributed to Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, avoids multiplying entities unnecessarily.

Guided Path
1
Identify the attributes mentioned
The poet enumerates attributes like speech, hearing, sight, and then says God is the Agent and Chooser. Notice that he mentions attributes of meaning (knowledge, life, power, will) through inference.
The poet's line itself does not explicitly list all seven attributes; the commentary fills in the details.
2
Understand the omission
The commentator points out that the poet did not mention the attribute states (e.g., 'being Knowing' or 'being Powerful'). These states necessarily accompany the attributes of meaning.
3
Learn the Ash'ari justification
The correct view, according to Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari, is that these states are not additional real entities. They are simply ways of saying that the attributes subsist in the essence.
This avoids the problem of positing an infinite regress of attributes.
4
Connect to faith and submission
The passage also emphasizes that believing in God as the sole agent and submitting to His commands is obligatory, tying theology to practice.
The lesson is not just theoretical; it has practical implications.

سبب عدم ذكر الناظم للصفات المعنوية
شرح الشيخ حسن محمد المشاط الملكي المالكي

His speech, hearing, and sight, so He is God, the Agent, the Chosen. Reason for the author not mentioning the attribute states. Explanation by Shaykh Hasan Muhammad al-Mashat al-Maliki al-Maliki. Then he branched on some of the attributes of meaning, which are knowledge, life, power, and will, by his saying: 'He is the One God, worshipped rightly, and He is the Agent, the Chosen; if He wills He does, and if He wills He leaves; no agent by nature nor by cause. This belief is obligatory upon the responsible person. And your Lord creates what He wills and chooses; they have no choice. Glory be to God and exalted is He above what they associate. And it is obligatory upon the responsible person to submit to the commands of God Almighty and avoid His prohibitions, for he, peace and blessings be upon him, said: 'He has tasted the taste of faith who is pleased with God as Lord, with Islam as religion, and with Muhammad as prophet.' and other such verses and hadiths indicating the abandonment of planning and delegation of affairs to the All-Knowing Expert. The people of knowledge said: 'Whoever does not plan, plans are made for him.' And refer to 'Al-Tanwir fi Isqat al-Tadbir' by my master Ibn Ata'Allah, may his secret be sanctified. Know that these seven attributes are those agreed upon, and the author did not mention the attribute states concomitant to the attributes of meaning, i.e., His being Knowing... etc., because the truth, as the commentator said, is what our imam, the imam of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a, Abu al-Hasan, may God be pleased with him, held: that they are not additional to the attributes of meaning, rather they are an expression of the subsistence of the attributes in the essence.

Commentary
This passage explains why the poet omitted the attribute states (الصفات المعنوية) in his poem. The poet's line mentions attributes of meaning: speech, hearing, sight, and then states that God is the sole Agent and Chooser. The commentator explains that the poet then branched on attributes of meaning like knowledge, life, power, and will. He emphasizes that God is the only true agent, not by nature or cause, and that this belief is obligatory. He cites Quran and Hadith to support the duty of submission and delegation of affairs. Then he addresses the omission: the poet did not mention the attribute states (like being Knowing, Powerful) that necessarily accompany the attributes of meaning. The commentator clarifies the correct Ash'ari position, quoting the commentator (al-Sharh) that Imam Abu al-Hasan al-Ash'ari held that these states are not additional entities, but rather are expressions of the fact that the attributes subsist in the essence. So they are not separate existents.

Attachment of Attributes and Its Classification
Defines ta'alluq and classifies the attachments of attributes of meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of ta'alluq (attachment) as it applies to God's attributes of meaning, which attributes require an object, and the special status of knowledge and speech in relating to all rational judgments.

Core Idea
Not all of God's attributes need an object; only life lacks attachment.

This passage from the commentary on al-Kharida defines a key term in the study of divine attributes: attachment (ta'alluq). It explains that each attribute of meaning (except life) necessarily requires an additional entity to which it relates. The passage then classifies attributes into those that attach and those that do not, and further divides the attaching attributes into three categories, with knowledge and speech falling into the first category—they attach to all three rational judgments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of ta'alluq
Read the definition carefully: attachment means the attribute requires something extra beyond its existence in the essence.
2
Identify the exception of life
Notice that life is singled out as not requiring attachment; it simply enables perception.
Life is an attribute of meaning but differs from others.
3
Classify the attributes
Follow the classification: attributes divide into those that attach and those that don't, and attaching attributes into three categories. Note that knowledge and speech attach to all rational judgments.
The passage only elaborates on the first category.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَوَاجِبٌ تَعْلِيقُ ذِي الصِّفَاتِ * حَتْمًا دَوَامَـا مَـا عَـدَا الْحَيَاةِ
شرح الشيخ حسن محمد المشاط الملكي المالكي
ثم شرع يُبين تعلّق كل من صفات المعاني والتعلق: هو اقتضاء الصفة أمرًا زائدا على قيامها بالذات، كاقتضاء العلم معلوما ينكشف به واقتضاء القدرة مقدورًا وهكذا، فقال
وواجب تعليق ذي أي هذه الصفات السابقة - صفات المعاني حتما دوامًا تأكيد لمعنى الوجوب، ودواما زيادة تأكيد لأن الواجب النقلي شأنه ذلك، ما عدا الحياة فإنها لا تتعلق بشيء، إذ هي صفة تصحح لمن قامت به الإدراك، من غير أن تطلب زائدا على قيامها بمحلها، وقد عرفت أن هذا هو معنى التعلق، فيجب على كل مكلف أن يعتقد ذلك.
واعلم أن هذه الصفات تنقسم:
إلى ما يتعلق.
وإلى ما لا يتعلق.
والذي يتعلق ينقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام:
قسم يتعلق بجميع أقسام الحكم العقلي: الواجب، والجائز، والمستحيل، وهما صفتا العلم والكلام.
وشرح الخريدة، وهذه الصفات السبعة هي المتفق عليها، وزاد بعضهم صفة الإدراك، والحق التوقف فيها.
And obligatory is the attachment of these attributes, necessarily and perpetually, except for life. Explanation by Shaykh Hasan Muhammad al-Mashat al-Maliki al-Maliki. Then he began to explain the attachment of each of the attributes of meaning. And attachment is: the attribute requiring something additional beyond its subsistence in the essence, such as knowledge requiring a known thing to be disclosed by it, and power requiring a thing that is empowered, and so on. So he said: And obligatory is the attachment of 'these' meaning these preceding attributes—attributes of meaning—necessarily and perpetually as an emphasis of the meaning of obligation, and perpetually as extra emphasis because the transmitted obligation is like that, except for life, for it does not attach to anything, since it is an attribute that validates for the one in whom it subsists perception, without demanding anything additional beyond its subsistence in its locus. And you have known that this is the meaning of attachment, so it is obligatory upon every responsible person to believe that. Know that these attributes are divided: into what attaches and what does not attach. And what attaches is divided into three categories: a category that attaches to all divisions of rational judgment: the obligatory, the possible, and the impossible, and these are the two attributes of knowledge and speech. And Sharh al-Kharida: these seven attributes are the agreed upon ones, and some added the attribute of perception, and the truth is to refrain regarding it.
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies the 'attachment' (تعلق) of the attributes of meaning. The poet's line states that the attachment of these attributes is obligatory and perpetual except for life. The commentator explains that 'attachment' means the attribute requires something additional beyond its mere subsistence in the essence. For example, knowledge requires a known object, power requires an object of power. Then he comments on the poet's words: the 'obligatory attachment' of these attributes (except life) is emphasized with 'necessarily and perpetually'. Life is excluded because it does not attach to anything; it merely validates perception for its locus. The passage then provides a classification: attributes are divided into those that attach and those that do not (like life). Those that attach are further divided into three categories, and the commentator specifically mentions the first category: the attributes of knowledge and speech attach to all three rational judgments (obligatory, possible, impossible). The final sentence notes that some add the attribute of perception, but the correct position is to refrain (tawaqquf).

صفات المعاني
الحكم العقلي
الحياة
العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think every attribute of meaning requires an external object or relation.
Correction: Life is an exception; it does not require attachment because it enables perception without needing an object beyond its existence.

Checkpoint

A) Life and will.

Key Takeaway
Ta'alluq is the attribute's requirement for an additional object; life uniquely lacks this requirement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
God’s knowledge attaches to every thought, word, and deed of yours—even those you keep hidden. When you truly internalize that His knowledge encompasses the obligatory, possible, and impossible, what does that mean for the secrets you guard in your heart today? Will you bring one hidden fear or sin before Him in sincere repentance right now?
?
The attribute of life in God requires no object—it simply is, without need or attachment. In contrast, your life is filled with attachments to people, possessions, and status. Which attachment are you clinging to that actually distances you from living in the awareness of God’s presence? What is one step you can take today to release that attachment and live more freely before Him?
?
God’s speech (kalam) attaches to the impossible, meaning He speaks truth about what cannot be—like the impossibility of a partner. Your own speech often dwells on what you think can or cannot be, yet it is limited and fallible. How does the vastness of God’s speech challenge the words you speak carelessly? Will you commit today to speaking only what is true and beneficial, knowing that God hears every word?

Knowledge and Speech Relate to All Rational Judgments
Al-Mashat explains the relation of knowledge and speech to all rational judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine knowledge and speech relate to the three rational judgments, and the difference between the relation of disclosure (inkishaf) and the relation of indication (dalala).

Core Idea
Divine knowledge discloses; divine speech indicates both relate to all rational judgments eternally.

This passage from Al-Mashat's commentary clarifies how two key divine attributes—knowledge and speech—relate to the entire spectrum of rational judgments. We have seen that rational judgments are divided into necessary, possible, and impossible. Now we learn that both divine knowledge and divine speech encompass all these categories, but each in its own distinct manner.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the commentator explains the poet's line about knowledge and speech relating to 'all the divisions'.
The phrase 'all the divisions' refers to the three rational judgments.
2
Identify the two modes of relation
The passage distinguishes between the relation of disclosure (for knowledge) and the relation of indication (for speech). Understand how these differ: disclosure entails a known object being unveiled, while indication entails a meaning being conveyed.
3
Reflect on the eternal scope
Notice that both attributes are described as relating to all universals and particulars eternally, without reflection or deduction. This guards against thinking Allah's knowledge is acquired or His speech temporary.
This connects to the common misunderstanding mentioned.
4
Understand unity and multiplicity
The passage concludes that Allah is one in essence, but the multiplicity of attributes arises from the diversity of their relations, like the example of knowledge and power. This is a key Ash'ari principle.
This explains how many attributes can be affirmed without compromising divine unity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإلى ذلك كله أشار الناظم بقوله: (فالعلم جزما) بالنصب معمول لقوله بعد: «تعلقا»، (والكلام السامي) العالي المتنزه عما لا يليق به، (تعلقا) أي تعلقا جزمًا، أي مقطوعا به بسائر الأقسام للحكم العقلي: الواجب والجائز والمستحيل، لأنهما يتطلبان أمرا زائدا على القيام بمحلها. أما العلم فإنه يتعلق تعلق انكشاف ويقتضي معلوما ينكشف به، وأما الكلام فإنه يتعلق تعلق دلالة ويقتضي معنى يدل عليه، فعلم الله تعالى يتعلق بجميع الكليات والجزئيات أزلا وأبدًا، بلا تأمل ولا استدلال، والكلام يدل على ما ذكر دلالة مستمرة بلا انقطاع أزلا وأبدًا، فهو تعالى آمر ناه مخيّر، فهو في نفسه واحد، وتكثره إنما هو بحسب التعلقات كالعلم والقدرة.
And to all of that the poet alluded by his statement: 'For knowledge, certainly, and the sublime speech — they relate to all the divisions.' (al-ilm jazman) is in the accusative as the operative of what follows: 'they relate', (and al-kalam al-sami) the lofty, transcendent above what does not befit Him, (tallaqa) i.e., they relate certainly, meaning definitely, to all the divisions of rational judgment: the necessary, the possible, and the impossible, because they require an additional matter beyond their subsistence in their locus. As for knowledge, it relates with a relation of disclosure and requires a known object that is disclosed by it. As for speech, it relates with a relation of indication and requires a meaning that it indicates. So the knowledge of Allah the Exalted relates to all universals and particulars, eternally and perpetually, without reflection or deduction. And speech indicates what was mentioned with a continuous indication without interruption, eternally and perpetually. So He the Exalted is commanding, forbidding, and giving choice. He is one in Himself, and its multiplicity is only according to the relations, like knowledge and power.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the poet's line about knowledge and speech. The poet's line says that knowledge and speech relate to 'all the divisions', and the commentator explains that this refers to the three rational judgments: the necessary (wajib), the possible (ja'iz), and the impossible (mustahil). Both knowledge and speech require something additional beyond their own subsistence in the divine essence. Knowledge has a relation of disclosure (inkishaf) — it entails a known object that becomes disclosed through it. Speech has a relation of indication (dalala) — it entails a meaning that it indicates. Therefore, Allah's knowledge encompasses all universals and particulars from eternity to eternity without any need for reflection or inference. Similarly, Allah's speech indicates continuously without interruption from eternity to eternity. Consequently, Allah is one who commands, forbids, and gives choice. The text clarifies that although the divine attributes are one in His essence, their multiplicity arises from the diversity of their relations, just like knowledge and power.

Key Terms
العلم (knowledge)

Key Takeaway

Warning Against Misrepresenting Divine Knowledge
Al-Mashat presents a warning from Ahmad Zarruq about misrepresenting divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ahmad Zarruq forbade certain expressions about Allah's knowledge and how this protects divine transcendence.

Core Idea
Speaking about Allah's knowledge requires guarding against accidentalist or anthropomorphic implications.

Ahmad Zarruq, a renowned scholar, issued a clear warning about how we speak of Allah's knowledge. In this lesson, we will examine his admonition and the theological reasoning behind it. This passage underscores the importance of precise language in matters of divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the English translation. Notice the structure: a warning (tanbih) from a specific scholar.
The passage begins with 'تنبیهان الأول' indicating the first of two warnings.
2
Identify the prohibited statements
Locate the phrases that Zarruq forbids: saying Allah's knowledge is an 'arad (accident) or jism (body), or that He knows through something in His essence or a locus.
3
Understand the reason for prohibition
The passage gives the reason: such statements 'yujib takyif' and 'tahdid' (qualify and delimit) Allah's knowledge. Explain these terms in your own words.
Takyif means assigning a how or quality, tahdid means limiting or confining.
4
Reflect on the broader principle
Consider why divine attributes cannot be described in terms of created things. This lesson guards against accidentalist or anthropomorphic thinking about Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبیهان الأول: منع سيدي أحمد زَرُّوق أن يقال: إن علم الله تعالى عرض أو جسم، أو أنه سبحانه وتعالى يعلم في ذاته أو في محل، لأن ذلك يوجب تكييف علم الله تعالى وتحديده، وعلم الله تعالى ليس كذلك. والله أعلم.
Two admonitions. The first: Master Ahmad Zarruq forbade that it be said: 'Indeed, the knowledge of Allah the Exalted is an accident or a body, or that He the Glorified and Exalted knows by a known thing in His essence or in a locus, because that necessitates qualifying and delimiting the knowledge of Allah the Exalted, and the knowledge of Allah the Exalted is not like that. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is the first of two admonitions (tanbihan) from the commentator. It quotes the respected scholar Ahmad Zarruq, who issues a prohibition regarding how one speaks about Allah's knowledge. Specifically, it is forbidden to say that Allah's knowledge is an 'arad (accident) or a jism (body), or that Allah knows through something that exists in His essence or in a locus. Such statements would imply that Allah's knowledge is subject to qualification (takyeef) and delimitation (tahdid), which are attributes of contingent beings. The knowledge of Allah, being an eternal divine attribute, is not like that. The admonition thus guards against anthropomorphic or accidentalist conceptions of divine knowledge, emphasizing its transcendence.

Key Terms
عرض (accident)
جسم (body)
تكييف (qualifying)
تحديد (delimiting)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Because that implies qualifying and delimiting Allah's knowledge
C) Because accidents and bodies are created
D) Because Allah does not have knowledge

Key Takeaway
One must avoid any expression that would qualify or delimit Allah's knowledge, as it is unlike any created attribute.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

How Divine Knowledge and Speech Relate to All Things
The author explains how divine knowledge and speech relate to all categories, with distinct modes of relation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how divine knowledge and speech relate to all categories of rational judgment and the nature of their relation (disclosure vs indication).

Core Idea
Divine knowledge and speech are not blank—they inherently relate to all things.

In this passage, the author explains that Allah's attributes of knowledge and speech are not passive; they inherently relate to all possible categories of existence—the necessary, possible, and impossible. Understanding this relation helps us grasp how God knows and communicates about everything without change in His essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the poetic line and its prose commentary.
2
Identify the two kinds of relations
The passage mentions two kinds: one relating to all possible things (attributes of power and will) and one relating to all existent things (hearing and sight). Find these in the text.
This shows a classification of divine attributes based on their objects.
3
Understand disclosure vs indication
The passage distinguishes knowledge's relation (disclosure) from speech's relation (indication). Ask yourself: what does each require?
Knowledge requires a known object to be disclosed; speech requires a meaning to be indicated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالعِلْمُ جَزْمًا والكَلامُ السَّامِي * تَعَلَّقَا بِسَائِرِ الأَقْسَامِ
وقسم يتعلق بجميع الممكنات، وهما صفتا القدرة، والإرادة. وقسم يتعلق بجميع الموجودات، وهما صفتا السمع والبصر. و إلى ذلك كله أشار الناظم بقوله: (فالعلم جزما) بالنصب معمول لقوله بعد: «تعلقا»، (والكلام (۱) السامي) العالي المتنزه عما لا يليق به، (تعلقا) أي تعلقا جزمًا، أي مقطوعا به بسائر الأقسام للحكم العقلي: الواجب والجائز والمستحيل، لأنهما يتطلبان أمرا زائدا على القيام بمحلها. أما العلم فإنه يتعلق تعلق انكشاف ويقتضي معلوما ينكشف به، وأما الكلام فإنه يتعلق تعلق دلالة ويقتضي معنى يدل عليه، فعلم الله تعالى يتعلق بجميع الكليات والجزئيات أزلا وأبدًا، بلا تأمل ولا استدلال، والكلام يدل على ما ذكر دلالة مستمرة بلا انقطاع أزلا وأبدًا، فهو تعالى آمر ناه مخيّر، فهو في نفسه واحد، وتكثره إنما هو بحسب التعلقات كالعلم والقدرة.
So knowledge, certainly, and the lofty speech relate to all categories. And a kind relates to all possible things, and they are the attributes of power and will. And a kind relates to all existent things, and they are the attributes of hearing and sight. And to all that the versifier points by his saying: 'So knowledge, certainly, and the lofty speech relate' — 'knowledge' is in the accusative as the object of the later word 'relate', and 'the lofty speech' meaning the high, transcendent above what is unbecoming to it, 'related' meaning related certainly, i.e., decisively, to all categories of the rational judgment: the necessary, the possible, and the impossible, because they require an additional matter beyond subsisting in their locus. As for knowledge, it relates with a relation of disclosure and requires a known thing to be disclosed by, and as for speech, it relates with a relation of indication and requires a meaning to be indicated. Thus the knowledge of Allah the Exalted relates to all universals and particulars eternally and forever, without reflection or deduction, and speech indicates what was mentioned with continuous indication without break eternally and forever. So He, Exalted, is Commander, Prohibitor, and Chooser. He is in Himself one, and its multiplicity is only according to the relations, like knowledge and power.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the line of poetry stating that divine knowledge and speech relate to all categories. The author first explains two types of relations: one to all possible things (attributes of power and will) and one to all existent things (hearing and sight). Then he explains the line in detail: 'knowledge' and 'speech' are accusative as they are governed by 'relate'. He emphasizes that both attributes require an additional relational dimension beyond their existence in the essence. Knowledge relates by disclosure (inkishaf) and requires a known object; speech relates by indication (dalala) and requires a meaning. He affirms that God's knowledge encompasses all universals and particulars, without reflection or deduction, eternally. His speech indicates continuously. God is one in essence but multiple in relations, like knowledge and power.

Key Terms
العلم
الكلام
التعلق
الانكشاف
الدلالة
الواجب
الجائز
المستحيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that knowledge and speech are bare attributes without any relational aspect or object.
Correction: The passage clarifies that both attributes inherently involve a relation: knowledge requires disclosure of a known object, and speech requires indication of a meaning.

Checkpoint

A) It relates by indication.
B) It relates by disclosure.
C) It relates by will.
D) It relates by power.

Key Takeaway
Divine knowledge and speech are relationally rich: knowledge discloses all realities, speech indicates commands and prohibitions, both eternal and comprehensive.

Correct Formulation of God's Knowledge: Detailed not General
Zarruq prohibits two formulations and mandates the correct one regarding Allah's knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why it is incorrect to say that Allah's knowledge is general, and how to properly affirm His detailed knowledge.

Core Idea
Allah's knowledge is not general; He knows each thing in detail.

In this short but significant note, Imam Zarruq addresses a common but problematic way of describing Allah's knowledge. He forbids saying it is 'general' because that implies imperfection. Let us examine the correct formulation that preserves divine perfection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Notice the concise, authoritative tone of a ruling.
This is a direct prohibition and prescription from a respected scholar.
2
Identify the prohibited formulations
Find two statements that Zarruq forbids: saying knowledge relates 'in general', and saying it relates 'in general and in detail' together.
3
Understand why 'general' is problematic
Reflect on why 'general' suggests imperfection. If knowledge is only general, it implies lack of detailed awareness. Even adding 'and detailed' creates a contradiction.
God's knowledge is simple and all-encompassing; it cannot be both general and detailed in a composite sense.
4
Memorize the correct formulation
Learn the required statement: 'يتعلق بها تفصيلا' (It relates to them in detail). This affirms that God knows every particular thing individually.
This is the precise wording that avoids any misrepresentation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأول: منع سيدي أحمد زَرُّوق أن يقال: إن علم الله تعالى يتعلق بالمعلومات إجمالا لإبهامه أنه لا يتعلق بها تفصيلا، كما منع أن يقال: يتعلق بها إجمالاً وتفصيلا للتناقض، وأوجب أن يقال: يتعلق بها تفصيلًا.
The first: Sidi Ahmad Zarruq prohibited that it be said: 'Indeed the knowledge of Allah the Exalted relates to the known things in a general way' because it suggests that it does not relate to them in detail, just as he prohibited that it be said: 'It relates to them in a general and detailed way' due to contradiction, and he required that it be said: 'It relates to them in detail.'
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a note (tanbih) recording a ruling from Ahmad Zarruq on how to describe God's knowledge. He forbids saying that God's knowledge relates to things 'in general' (ijmalan) because that implies it does not relate in detail (tafsilan). He also forbids saying 'in general and in detail' together because this is contradictory. Instead, one must affirm that God's knowledge relates to things 'in detail' (tafsilan), meaning He knows each particular thing individually.

Key Terms
إجمالا
تفصيلا
علم الله
زروق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think saying 'general knowledge' is acceptable as a linguistic convenience or that it is not a serious theological error.
Correction: Zarruq explicitly prohibits it because it suggests Allah does not know details, which is a deficiency in divine knowledge.

Checkpoint
According to Zarruq, which formulation for God's knowledge is correct?
A) It relates to things in general.
B) It relates in general and in detail.
C) It relates in detail.
D) It relates only to universals.

Key Takeaway
Allah's knowledge is detailed, not general; affirm it as such to avoid implying imperfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Meaning of God's Knowledge of the Impossible
Al-Siba'i explains God's knowledge of impossible things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's knowledge of the impossible is knowledge of its impossibility, not a mental representation of a possible content.

Core Idea
God knows the impossible as impossible.

In this lesson, we explore a subtle but important point about divine knowledge: how does God know things that are impossible? The scholar al-Siba'i clarifies that God's knowledge of the impossible is simply His knowledge that it cannot happen. This teaching, rooted in the Salaf's statement, helps distinguish divine omniscience from human limitation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage is a second note (الثاني) continuing a previous discussion.
2
Understand the definition
Al-Siba'i says the meaning of God's knowledge of the impossible is His knowledge of its impossibility. This is the core idea.
3
Note the consequence of imagining occurrence
If the impossible were imagined to occur, corruption (فساد) would result. This shows why it is impossible.
This helps differentiate between what is impossible and what is merely unknown.
4
Recognize the Salaf's expression
The Salaf summarised it as: 'He knows what was, what will be, and what has not been — if it were, how it would be.' This includes hypotheticals.
5
Appreciate the distinction from human knowledge
Humans may not grasp the full impossibility of something, but God knows it perfectly. This distinguishes divine knowledge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: قال العلامة السباعي: معنى تعلق علمه تعالى بالمستحيل: علمه تعالى باستحالته، وأنه لو تصور وقوعه لزم منه الفساد، وهذا مما أشار له بعض السلف بقوله: «علم ماكان، وعلم ما يكون، وعلم ما لم يكن إن لو كان كيف يكون»، وهذا يميز علمه تعالى عن علمنا بالمستحيل، والله أعلم.
The second: Al-Siba'i said: The meaning of His knowledge, Exalted, relating to the impossible: His knowledge, Exalted, of its impossibility, and that if its occurrence were conceived, corruption would necessarily result. And this is what some Salaf indicated by their saying: 'He knows what was, and what will be, and what has not been — if it were, how it would be.' And this distinguishes His knowledge, Exalted, from our knowledge of the impossible. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 67

Commentary
This passage is a second note explaining the nature of God's knowledge of the impossible. The scholar al-Siba'i clarifies that God's knowledge of an impossible thing is simply His knowledge that it is impossible. If its occurrence were imagined, it would lead to contradiction (fasād). The Salaf expressed this by saying God knows what was, what will be, and what would have been if it were to occur. This distinguishes divine knowledge from human knowledge: humans may not know the full implications of impossibility, but God knows it perfectly.

Key Terms
المستحيل
الاستحالة
الفساد
السلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God mentally entertains impossible scenarios as possible.
Correction: The passage clarifies God knows the impossibility, not a possible content.

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of God's knowledge of the impossible according to al-Siba'i?
A) God imagines what the impossible would be like.
B) God knows the impossibility of the impossible and its consequences.
C) God knows the impossible as a possible thing.
D) God does not know the impossible.

Key Takeaway
God's knowledge of the impossible is knowledge of its impossibility, not a representation of a possible event.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The Salaf's phrase includes 'what has not been — if it were, how it would be.' Are you willing to accept that God knows the full consequences of every hypothetical scenario, while you cannot? What does that mean for your arrogance about your own knowledge? What practical change in your attitude toward uncertainty will you make after this lesson?

Divine Power and Will Encompass All Possible Things
The author explains that divine power and will encompass all possible things, refuting the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine power and will (qudra and irada) extend to all possible things, including human voluntary actions, and why the Mu'tazila position is considered contrary to piety.

Core Idea
God's power and will cover everything possible — no human action is outside them.

This lesson focuses on a key point of Sunni creed: that God's attributes of power and will are universal in scope. The author contrasts this with the Mu'tazila claim that humans create their own actions independently. Understanding this distinction is crucial for grasping the Islamic doctrine of qadar (divine decree) and refuting deterministic or libertarian extremes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the poetic line and the commentary structure.
2
Identify the main claim
The passage states that power and will relate to all possible things. This is the central dogma being upheld.
The author says this formulation is more concise than al-Laqani's.
3
Understand the Mu'tazila position
The author explains the Mu'tazila claim: God's power does not relate to voluntary actions, and sins are not willed by God because will would necessitate command.
The Mu'tazila use the idea that will implies command to argue God cannot will sin.
4
Connect to 'brother of piety'
The phrase 'O brother of piety' indicates that opposing this creed (like the Mu'tazila) is inconsistent with true piety.
This is a rhetorical device to emphasize the importance of correct belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقدرة إرادة تعلقا * بالممكنات كلها هذا أخصر وأوضح من قول البرهان اللقاني: فقدرةً بممكن تعلّقت تعلّقت (۲) بِلا تَنَاهِي مَا بِهِ يا أخا التقى المصاحب والملازم لامتثال أوامر الله، واجتناب نواهيه، وفيه إشارة إلى أن من خالف هذه العقيدة كالمعتزلة ليسوا من أهل التّقى، فإنهم قالوا: إن قدرته تعالى لا تتعلق بأفعال العبد الاختيارية، بل العبد مستقل بخلق فعله الاختياري، وإن بعض أفعاله الاختيارية كالمعاصي ليست بإرادة الله تعالى بناء على أن الإرادة تستلزم الأمر،
And power and will related to all possible things. This is more concise and clear than the statement of Burhan al-Laqani: 'So power related to a possible thing, related without end, O brother of piety.' The brother of piety is the companion and adherent to obeying the commands of Allah and avoiding His prohibitions. And in it is an indication that whoever opposes this creed, like the Mu'tazila, is not among the people of piety. For they said: Indeed His power, Exalted, does not relate to the voluntary actions of the servant, but rather the servant is independent in creating his own voluntary action, and that some of his voluntary actions, like sins, are not by the will of Allah, Exalted, based on the idea that will necessitates command.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the line of poetry stating that power and will relate to all possible things. The author notes that this formulation is more concise than al-Laqani's. He then explains that the phrase 'O brother of piety' indicates that those who oppose this doctrine, specifically the Mu'tazila, are not truly pious. The Mu'tazila claim that God's power does not extend to human voluntary actions; instead, humans create their own actions independently. They also claim that some actions like sins are not willed by God because, in their view, will necessitates command (i.e., if God willed it, He would command it, but He commands against sin). The commentary refutes this by implication, affirming that God's power and will encompass all actions, including human voluntary acts.

Key Terms
القدرة
الإرادة
الممكنات
المعتزلة
الأفعال الاختيارية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power and will do not extend to human voluntary actions, particularly sins.
Correction: According to Sunni creed, God's power and will encompass all possible things, including all human actions, good and bad.

Checkpoint
What do the Mu'tazila claim about God's power and will regarding human voluntary actions?
A) They affirm that God's power extends to all human actions.
B) They deny that God's power relates to voluntary actions; humans create their own.
C) They say God's will is identical to His command.
D) They say God does not know human actions.

Key Takeaway
Divine power and will encompass all possible things, including human voluntary actions, and the Mu'tazila position is inconsistent with piety.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

God's Power and Will Encompass All Possible Things
Commentary on a verse affirming God's power and will over all possibles, with refutation of Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's power and will extend to every possible thing, including human actions, and how this refutes the Mu'tazilite position.

Core Idea
God's power and will are universal, covering all contingent things without exception.

This passage from al-Bahja al-Saniyya explains a key verse affirming the comprehensiveness of God's power and will. The commentator, Shaykh Hasan al-Mashat, clarifies a nuance about God's knowledge and then directly refutes the Mu'tazilite view that human acts are independent of God's will. By the end, you will see how this verse serves as a clear statement of Sunni belief.

Guided Path
1
Read the verse and commentary
First, carefully read the Arabic verse and the commentary that follows. The verse is simple: 'And power and will attach to all contingent things.' The commentary expands on this.
Notice the commentator begins with a clarification about God's knowledge before addressing the verse.
2
Understand the knowledge clarification
The commentator explains that God's knowledge attaches to things both generally and in detail, contrary to a claim that it attaches only generally. He also discusses knowledge of the impossible.
This sets the theological framework for understanding divine attributes.
3
Appreciate the verse's clarity
The commentator praises the verse as more concise and clear than an alternative formulation by al-Laqani. Note the implicit call to piety in addressing the reader as 'O pious one.'
4
Identify the Mu'tazilite error
The passage explicitly refutes the Mu'tazila who claim God's power and will do not extend to human free acts. Understand their reasoning and why it is considered corrupt.
The commentator states this belief has already been proven corrupt earlier.

"And power and will attach to all contingent things, O pious one." Commentary of Shaykh Hasan Muhammad al-Mashat al-Maliki al-Maliki: "It attaches to known things generally, because of its ambiguity that it does not attach to them in detail, just as he forbade saying: 'It attaches to them generally and in detail' due to contradiction, and required that one say: 'It attaches to them in detail.' Second: al-Allama al-Siba'i said: The meaning of His knowledge, exalted, attaching to the impossible: His knowledge, exalted, of its impossibility, and that if its occurrence were imagined, corruption would necessarily follow from it. This is what some of the Salaf indicated by their saying: 'He knows what was, and He knows what will be, and He knows what was not, if it were to be, how it would be.' And this distinguishes His knowledge, exalted, from our knowledge of the impossible. And God knows best. (And power and will attach to all contingent things: this is more concise and clearer than the saying of al-Burhan al-Laqani: 'So power attaches to a contingent thing, attaches without limit to it.' O pious one, the companion and one who adheres to obeying God's commands and avoiding His prohibitions. And in it is an indication that those who oppose this belief, like the Mu'tazila, are not among the people of piety. For they said: His power, exalted, does not attach to the free acts of the servant, rather the servant is independent in creating his free act, and some of his free acts like sins are not by the will of God, exalted, based on the idea that will necessitates command. And it has already been stated that this belief is corrupt."
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the poetic verse "Wa qudratun iradatun ta'allaqa bi-l-mumkinati kulliha" (And power and will attach to all contingent things). The commentator, Shaykh Hasan al-Mashat, explains that the verse is affirming that God's power and will encompass all possible things. He first clarifies a nuance regarding God's knowledge: it attaches to things both generally and in detail, contrary to a claim that it only attaches generally. Then he quotes al-Siba'i on the meaning of God's knowledge of the impossible—it is knowledge of its impossibility. The commentator then praises the verse as clearer than an alternative formulation by al-Laqani. He points out that the verse contains an implicit rebuke of the Mu'tazila, who deny that God's power and will extend to human free actions, claiming instead that humans independently create their own acts and that sins are not willed by God because will necessitates command. The commentator concludes that this Mu'tazilite belief has already been shown to be corrupt earlier in the text.

Correction: God's power and will encompass all possible things, including human actions. Humans do not act independently; their actions are within God's power and will.

The Order of Attachment: Power Follows Will, Will Follows Knowledge
Sanusi clarifies the hierarchical attachment of divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hierarchical relationship among God's knowledge, will, and power, and why power depends on will and will on knowledge.

Core Idea
God's power always acts in accordance with His will, which always aligns with His knowledge.

This lesson explores the inner logic of divine action. The author explains that God's attributes do not operate in isolation; rather, they follow a precise order. Before we can understand how God creates or commands, we must see how knowledge, will, and power are interrelated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the hierarchical language.
Notice the words 'تابع' (dependent) and 'ترتيب' (order).
2
Identify the three attributes and their dependencies
The passage mentions power, will, and knowledge. Note how each one depends on the next. Write down the chain.
3
Understand the meaning of the chain
The meaning is that God does not create unless He wills, and He does not will unless He knows. This shows that God's actions are not arbitrary.
This explains why God's power is not independent.
4
Reflect on the example of faith and disbelief
The example shows that if God knows someone will persist in disbelief, He does not will faith for that person, so faith does not occur. This illustrates the hierarchy.
The example grounds the abstract principle.
5
Consider the theological significance
This order ensures that God's actions are perfectly rational and just, based on His knowledge and will.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترتيب تعلق واعلم أن تعلق القدرة والإرادة والعلم مرتب، فتعلق القدرة تابع لتعلق الإرادة، وتعلق الإرادة تابع لتعلق العلم.
فالمعنى: أنه تعالى لا يُوجد شيئًا أو يُعْدِمه إلا إذا أراده، ولا يريد إلا إذا علمه، فما علم أنه يكون أراد كونه ووجوده، ثم أبرزه على طبق الإرادة، وما عَلِم أنه لا يكون ولم يرد كونه فلا يوجد وإن أمر به، كالإيمان ممن عَلِم الله أنه يستمر على الكفر حتى الموت.
The order of attachment: Know that the attachment of power, will, and knowledge is ordered: the attachment of power is dependent on the attachment of will, and the attachment of will is dependent on the attachment of knowledge. So the meaning is: He, exalted, does not bring into existence anything or annihilate it unless He wills it, and He does not will unless He knows it. So what He knows will be, He wills its being and existence, then He brings it forth in accordance with the will. And what He knows will not be and He did not will its being, it does not exist even if He commands it, like faith in one whom God knows will persist in disbelief until death.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage explains the order of attachment among God's attributes of knowledge, will, and power. It is a classification: the attachment of power is subordinate to the attachment of will, and the attachment of will is subordinate to the attachment of knowledge. In other words, God does not create or annihilate anything unless He wills it, and He does not will anything unless He knows it. The commentator provides an example: if God knows that a person will persist in disbelief until death, He does not will that person's faith, and thus faith does not occur for that person, even if God commands it. This hierarchy underscores that God's action is perfectly ordered by His knowledge and will.

Why God’s Power and Will Do Not Affect the Necessary or Impossible
Proof that God's power and will do not affect the necessary or impossible, using the nature of effect.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God’s power and will are only attached to possible things, not to the necessary or impossible, and why this reflects perfection, not deficiency.

Core Idea
God’s power only affects what can change; the necessary and impossible are beyond effect.

This lesson explores a subtle but important point in Islamic theology: why God’s attributes of power and will do not extend to the necessary (wajib) or the impossible (mustahil). It might seem limiting, but the reasoning shows it is a mark of perfection. By examining the nature of effect and the logical consequences of attaching power to the impossible, we see that only possible things can be objects of divine action. This argument relies on two key principles: tahsil al-hasil (acquisition of the already acquired) and qalb al-haqa'iq (reversal of realities).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the author's concise style; every phrase serves the proof.
2
Identify the key terms and definitions
Note how the author defines power and will as 'attributes of effect' (صفتي تأثير) and explains the necessary concomitant of an effect.
This definition drives the entire proof.
3
Understand the two logical consequences
For the necessary, attaching power leads to tahsil al-hasil (if bringing into existence) or qalb al-haqa'iq (if annihilating). For the impossible, the consequences are reversed. Grasp why each is absurd.
Tahsil al-hasil means 'obtaining what is already obtained' – a logical contradiction.
4
Appreciate the conclusion and clarification
The passage concludes that God's perfection lies in this non-attachment, and clarifies it is due to the recipient (the necessary and impossible) not the agent.
This guards against misunderstanding God's power as limited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سبب عدم تعلق ومفهوم كلام الناظم رحمه الله تعالى: أن القدرة والإرادة لا تتعلقان بالواجب، ولا بالمستحيل، وهو كذلك، لأن القدرة والإرادة لما كانت صفتي تأثير، ومن لازم الأثر وجوده بعد عدم لزم أن ما لا يقبل العدم أصلا وهو الواجب، وما لا يقبل الوجود أصلا وهو المستحيل، لا يصح أن يكون أثرًا للقدرة والإرادة وإلا لزم تحصيل الحاصل على تعلقها بالواجب، بأن تعلقت بإيجاده، ولزم قلب الحقائق إن تعلقت بإعدامه. وكذا يقال في المستحيل يلزم على تعلقها به قلب الحقائق إن تعلقت بإيجاده وتحصل الحاصل إن تعلقت بإعدامه، والجميع محال وباطل، وما أدى إلى ذلك باطل، فالكمال المطلق لله عَزَوَجَلَّ في عدم تعلقها بالواجب والمستحيل.
وليكن منك على بال أن عدم تعلق القدرة والإرادة بما عدا الممكن ليس ذلك لقصور في الفاعل جَلَّ وَعَلَا، بل لنقص في القابل، فإدخال
The reason for non-attachment. And the meaning of the poet's statement, may God have mercy on him: that power and will do not attach to the necessary nor to the impossible, and it is so. Because power and will, since they are attributes of effect, and from the necessary concomitants of an effect is its existence after non-existence, it necessarily follows that what does not admit non-existence at all—that is the necessary—and what does not admit existence at all—that is the impossible—cannot be an effect of power and will. Otherwise, it would entail the acquisition of the already acquired if they attached to the necessary by bringing it into existence, and it would entail the reversal of realities if they attached to annihilating it. And likewise is said regarding the impossible: it would entail the reversal of realities if they attached to bringing it into existence, and the acquisition of the already acquired if they attached to annihilating it. And all of that is impossible and false, and whatever leads to that is false. So the absolute perfection of God, mighty and majestic, lies in the non-attachment of power and will to the necessary and impossible. And let it be well known to you that the non-attachment of power and will to other than the possible is not due to a deficiency in the agent, glory be to Him, but rather a deficiency in the recipient. So entering...
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage provides the reason why God's power and will do not attach to the necessary (wajib) or the impossible (mustahil). It is a proof: power and will are attributes of effect (sifat ta'thir), and effect necessitates existence after non-existence. The necessary cannot cease to exist, and the impossible cannot exist. Hence, attaching power to the necessary to bring it into existence would be 'acquiring the already acquired' (tahsil al-hasil), and attaching it to annihilate the necessary would reverse realities. Similarly for the impossible. Therefore, the absolute perfection of God is shown by the fact that His power and will only attach to possible things. The commentator clarifies that this non-attachment is not due to any deficiency in God, but rather due to the nature of the recipient (the necessary and impossible). The final phrase 'فإدخال' likely begins another point, but the argument as given is complete.

Misconception & Correction

Correction: The passage clarifies that non-attachment is due to the nature of the impossible itself—it is not an object that can receive effect—so God's power remains absolute; the deficiency is in the recipient.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
God's power and will are perfect because they are only directed toward what is possible, avoiding logical absurdities like tahsil al-hasil and qalb al-haqa'iq.

The Ordered Relationship of God's Attributes
Defines the ordered tacha'luq and proves non-relation to necessary and impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the hierarchical order of God's power, will, and knowledge, and why they do not relate to the necessary and impossible.

Core Idea
God's power and will only relate to the possible, not to the necessary or impossible.

This lesson explores the ordered relationship of God's attributes of power, will, and knowledge. The author explains that power follows will, and will follows knowledge. He then proves that these attributes do not relate to the necessary (God) or the impossible, as that would lead to logical absurdities like acquiring what is already present or inverting realities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the sequence of ideas. Notice the logical progression from order to limits.
The passage contains both a definition and a proof.
2
Understand the order of tacha'luq
Identify that power's tacha'luq is subordinate to will, and will's tacha'luq is subordinate to knowledge. This reflects a causal hierarchy in divine action.
The meaning is clarified by examples: God only creates what He wills, and He only wills what He knows.
3
Grasp the limits of power and will
The author states that power and will do not relate to the necessary (wajib) or the impossible (mustahil). Understand why: they are attributes of effect, and effect requires existence after non-existence.
The necessary cannot cease to exist; the impossible cannot come to exist.
4
Reflect on the proof
The proof uses the concepts of tahsil al-hasil (acquiring what is already acquired) and qalb al-haqa'iq (inversion of realities). Relating power to the necessary would entail one of these absurdities.
The conclusion is that God's absolute perfection lies in non-relation to these two categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن تعلق القدرة والإرادة والعلم مرتب، فتعلق القدرة تابع لتعلق الإرادة، وتعلق الإرادة تابع لتعلق العلم. فالمعنى: أنه تعالى لا يُوجد شيئًا أو يُعْدِمه إلا إذا أراده، ولا يريد إلا إذا علمه، فما علم أنه يكون أراد كونه ووجوده، ثم أبرزه على طبق الإرادة، وما عَلِم أنه لا يكون ولم يرد كونه فلا يوجد وإن أمر به، كالإيمان ممن عَلِم الله أنه يستمر على الكفر حتى الموت. ومفهوم كلام الناظم رحمه الله تعالى: أن القدرة والإرادة لا تتعلقان بالواجب، ولا بالمستحيل، وهو كذلك، لأن القدرة والإرادة لما كانت صفتي تأثير، ومن لازم الأثر وجوده بعد عدم لزم أن ما لا يقبل العدم أصلا وهو الواجب، وما لا يقبل الوجود أصلا وهو المستحيل، لا يصح أن يكون أثرًا للقدرة والإرادة وإلا لزم تحصيل الحاصل على تعلقها بالواجب، بأن تعلقت بإيجاده، ولزم قلب الحقائق إن تعلقت بإعدامه. وكذا يقال في المستحيل يلزم على تعلقها به قلب الحقائق إن تعلقت بإيجاده وتحصل الحاصل إن تعلقت بإعدامه، والجميع محال وباطل، وما أدى إلى ذلك باطل، فالكمال المطلق لله عز وجل في عدم تعلقها بالواجب والمستحيل. وليكن منك على بال أن عدم تعلق القدرة والإرادة بما عدا الممكن ليس ذلك لقصور في الفاعل جل وعلا، بل لنقص في القابل، فإدخال الجسم الكبير كالجمل في ثقب صغير كسم الخياط مستحيل، لا تتعلق القدرة به لعدم قابليته في الجمل، لكن القادر العظيم إذا أراد صغر الجسم الكبير، وكبر الثقب الصغير حتى خلق له قابلية لذلك، فافهم.
Know that the tacha'luq (relation/engagement) of power, will, and knowledge is ordered: the tacha'luq of power is subordinate to the tacha'luq of will, and the tacha'luq of will is subordinate to the tacha'luq of knowledge. The meaning is: He, exalted, does not bring something into existence or annihilate it except if He wills it, and He does not will except if He knows it. So what He knows will be, He wills its being and existence, then He brings it forth in accordance with the will. And what He knows will not be and He does not will its being, then it does not exist, even if He commands it, like faith from one whom God knows will persist in disbelief until death. And the purport of the author's (may God have mercy on him) speech: that power and will do not relate to the necessary (al-wajib), nor to the impossible (al-mustahil), and it is so. Because power and will, since they are attributes of effect (sifat ta'thir), and from the necessary concomitant of the effect is its existence after non-existence, it necessarily follows that what does not accept non-existence at all – which is the necessary – and what does not accept existence at all – which is the impossible – cannot be an effect of power and will. Otherwise, there would follow tahsil al-hasil (acquisition of the acquired) upon their relation to the necessary by relating to its bringing into existence, and there would follow inversion of realities if they related to its annihilation. Likewise, it is said regarding the impossible: there would follow inversion of realities if they related to its bringing into existence, and tahsil al-hasil if they related to its annihilation. All of that is absurd and false, and what leads to that is false. So absolute perfection for God, mighty and majestic, lies in their non-relation to the necessary and impossible. And let it be known to you that the non-relation of power and will to other than the possible is not due to deficiency in the agent (al-fa'il), exalted and high, but rather due to deficiency in the receiver (al-qabil). For entering a large body, like a camel, into a small hole like a needle's eye is impossible; power does not relate to it due to its lack of receptivity in the camel. But the mighty powerful one, if He wills, can make the large body small and enlarge the small hole until He creates receptivity for it. So understand.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a combined definition and proof. It begins by stating that the tacha'luq (engagement/relation) of God's attributes of power (qudra), will (irada), and knowledge ('ilm) is ordered hierarchically: power follows will, and will follows knowledge. The meaning is that God does not create or annihilate anything unless He wills it, and He does not will anything unless He knows it. Thus, what God knows will occur, He wills its existence and then brings it into being according to His will. What God knows will not occur and He does not will, it does not exist even if He commands it (e.g., faith from someone He knows will die in disbelief). Then the author explains that power and will do not relate to the necessary (wajib) or the impossible (mustahil). The reason is that power and will are attributes of effect (ta'thir), and effect requires existence after non-existence. The necessary (God) does not accept non-existence, and the impossible does not accept existence. If power/will related to the necessary by bringing it into existence, that would be 'tahsil al-hasil' (acquiring what is already present); if by annihilating it, that would invert realities. Similarly for the impossible, relating power to its existence would invert realities, and to its non-existence would be tahsil al-hasil. Both are impossible, so it is perfect for God that power and will do not relate to them. Finally, the author clarifies that this non-relation is not due to deficiency in God (the agent) but due to deficiency in the receiver (the object). He gives the example of a camel entering a needle's eye: it is impossible due to lack of receptivity, not because God is incapable. But if God wills, He can change the object's receptivity. So understand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's power can relate to absolutely anything, including creating another god or making the necessary cease to exist.
Correction: God's power and will only relate to the possible. The necessary (God) cannot be affected, and the impossible cannot be actualized. Relating power to them would lead to logical absurdities like tahsil al-hasil or inversion of realities, which are incompatible with God's perfection.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the order of tacha'luq for power, will, and knowledge?
A) Power follows knowledge, knowledge follows will.
B) Power follows will, will follows knowledge.
C) Will follows power, power follows knowledge.
D) All three are independent of each other.

Key Takeaway
The tacha'luq of power and will is hierarchically ordered and limited to the possible, safeguarding God's perfection from logical absurdities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that God's will is perfectly ordered and does not relate to what is impossible or beyond its scope. Reflect on a time when you insisted that God should answer a prayer in a specific way, perhaps ignoring the signs that His will was otherwise. How does this ordered tacha'luq challenge you to submit to His knowledge and will, even when the outcome seems contrary to your desires? What needs to change in your du'a (supplication) to align with His perfect order?
?
If God's power and will only relate to what is possible (mumkin), then everything that happens is within the realm of possibility. Does this truth bring you comfort or unease? Considering that He knows all possible outcomes and wills only what is best, how does this affect your trust in His decree (qadr) when you face difficulties or disappointments? Are you willing to accept that some impossibilities (like changing the past or forcing belief) are not part of His plan, and that this refusal is actually a mark of His perfection?

The Role of the Receiver in Divine Power
Clarifies that non-relation of power to impossible is due to receiver deficiency, not agent deficiency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the non-relation of God's power to the impossible is due to the receiver's deficiency, not any limitation in God.

Core Idea
Impossibility lies in the receiver, not in the agent.

After establishing that power and will only relate to the possible, the author clarifies a potential misunderstanding. Some might think this implies a deficiency in God's power. The author explains that it is actually due to the deficiency in the receiver (the object). He gives the powerful example of a camel trying to pass through a needle's eye to illustrate this point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Note that this passage is a clarification and contains an illustrative example.
The passage directly addresses a potential misconception.
2
Understand the distinction
Identify the two possibilities: deficiency in the agent (God) vs. deficiency in the receiver (the object). The author emphasizes it is the latter.
This is crucial for understanding divine attributes.
3
Analyze the camel example
Reflect on why a camel cannot pass through a needle's eye: it is not because of lack of power, but because of the camel's size (lack of receptivity).
The example shows that the object must have the capacity to receive the action.
4
Apply the principle
Understand that God can change the receiver's receptivity if He wills, making the impossible possible by altering the object's nature.
This preserves God's absolute power while explaining why some things are impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن تعلق القدرة والإرادة والعلم مرتب، فتعلق القدرة تابع لتعلق الإرادة، وتعلق الإرادة تابع لتعلق العلم. فالمعنى: أنه تعالى لا يُوجد شيئًا أو يُعْدِمه إلا إذا أراده، ولا يريد إلا إذا علمه، فما علم أنه يكون أراد كونه ووجوده، ثم أبرزه على طبق الإرادة، وما عَلِم أنه لا يكون ولم يرد كونه فلا يوجد وإن أمر به، كالإيمان ممن عَلِم الله أنه يستمر على الكفر حتى الموت. ومفهوم كلام الناظم رحمه الله تعالى: أن القدرة والإرادة لا تتعلقان بالواجب، ولا بالمستحيل، وهو كذلك، لأن القدرة والإرادة لما كانت صفتي تأثير، ومن لازم الأثر وجوده بعد عدم لزم أن ما لا يقبل العدم أصلا وهو الواجب، وما لا يقبل الوجود أصلا وهو المستحيل، لا يصح أن يكون أثرًا للقدرة والإرادة وإلا لزم تحصيل الحاصل على تعلقها بالواجب، بأن تعلقت بإيجاده، ولزم قلب الحقائق إن تعلقت بإعدامه. وكذا يقال في المستحيل يلزم على تعلقها به قلب الحقائق إن تعلقت بإيجاده وتحصل الحاصل إن تعلقت بإعدامه، والجميع محال وباطل، وما أدى إلى ذلك باطل، فالكمال المطلق لله عز وجل في عدم تعلقها بالواجب والمستحيل. وليكن منك على بال أن عدم تعلق القدرة والإرادة بما عدا الممكن ليس ذلك لقصور في الفاعل جل وعلا، بل لنقص في القابل، فإدخال الجسم الكبير كالجمل في ثقب صغير كسم الخياط مستحيل، لا تتعلق القدرة به لعدم قابليته في الجمل، لكن القادر العظيم إذا أراد صغر الجسم الكبير، وكبر الثقب الصغير حتى خلق له قابلية لذلك، فافهم.
Know that the tacha'luq (relation/engagement) of power, will, and knowledge is ordered: the tacha'luq of power is subordinate to the tacha'luq of will, and the tacha'luq of will is subordinate to the tacha'luq of knowledge. The meaning is: He, exalted, does not bring something into existence or annihilate it except if He wills it, and He does not will except if He knows it. So what He knows will be, He wills its being and existence, then He brings it forth in accordance with the will. And what He knows will not be and He does not will its being, then it does not exist, even if He commands it, like faith from one whom God knows will persist in disbelief until death. And the purport of the author's (may God have mercy on him) speech: that power and will do not relate to the necessary (al-wajib), nor to the impossible (al-mustahil), and it is so. Because power and will, since they are attributes of effect (sifat ta'thir), and from the necessary concomitant of the effect is its existence after non-existence, it necessarily follows that what does not accept non-existence at all – which is the necessary – and what does not accept existence at all – which is the impossible – cannot be an effect of power and will. Otherwise, there would follow tahsil al-hasil (acquisition of the acquired) upon their relation to the necessary by relating to its bringing into existence, and there would follow inversion of realities if they related to its annihilation. Likewise, it is said regarding the impossible: there would follow inversion of realities if they related to its bringing into existence, and tahsil al-hasil if they related to its annihilation. All of that is absurd and false, and what leads to that is false. So absolute perfection for God, mighty and majestic, lies in their non-relation to the necessary and impossible. And let it be known to you that the non-relation of power and will to other than the possible is not due to deficiency in the agent (al-fa'il), exalted and high, but rather due to deficiency in the receiver (al-qabil). For entering a large body, like a camel, into a small hole like a needle's eye is impossible; power does not relate to it due to its lack of receptivity in the camel. But the mighty powerful one, if He wills, can make the large body small and enlarge the small hole until He creates receptivity for it. So understand.
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage is a combined definition and proof. It begins by stating that the tacha'luq (engagement/relation) of God's attributes of power (qudra), will (irada), and knowledge ('ilm) is ordered hierarchically: power follows will, and will follows knowledge. The meaning is that God does not create or annihilate anything unless He wills it, and He does not will anything unless He knows it. Thus, what God knows will occur, He wills its existence and then brings it into being according to His will. What God knows will not occur and He does not will, it does not exist even if He commands it (e.g., faith from someone He knows will die in disbelief). Then the author explains that power and will do not relate to the necessary (wajib) or the impossible (mustahil). The reason is that power and will are attributes of effect (ta'thir), and effect requires existence after non-existence. The necessary (God) does not accept non-existence, and the impossible does not accept existence. If power/will related to the necessary by bringing it into existence, that would be 'tahsil al-hasil' (acquiring what is already present); if by annihilating it, that would invert realities. Similarly for the impossible, relating power to its existence would invert realities, and to its non-existence would be tahsil al-hasil. Both are impossible, so it is perfect for God that power and will do not relate to them. Finally, the author clarifies that this non-relation is not due to deficiency in God (the agent) but due to deficiency in the receiver (the object). He gives the example of a camel entering a needle's eye: it is impossible due to lack of receptivity, not because God is incapable. But if God wills, He can change the object's receptivity. So understand.

إرادة
فاعل
قابل
مستحيل
قادر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If God's power does not relate to the impossible, then God is not all-powerful.
Correction: God's power is absolute; the impossible is impossible due to the receiver's deficiency, not God's. God can change the receiver's nature, making the formerly impossible possible.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does God's power not relate to the impossible?
A) Because God is unable to do it.
B) Because the object lacks the necessary receptivity.
C) Because God chooses not to do it.
D) Because it contradicts God's will.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of certain things does not indicate a limitation in God's power, but rather a lack of receptivity in the object, which God can change if He wills.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a situation that seems impossible to change, do you secretly attribute that impossibility to God's lack of power over it, or to your own lack of receptivity? How does this lesson expose the real obstacle in your life? This week, name one area where you have given up because you thought 'it's impossible' and ask yourself: Is this truly impossible for God, or have I simply assumed my current state cannot change? Then take one concrete step to alter your receptivity.
?
Have you ever used the phrase 'it's not meant to be' or 'God hasn't willed it' to excuse a failure or avoid effort? How can you distinguish between a genuine impossibility (due to receiver deficiency) and a test from God that requires you to change your own receptivity? Write down one area of your life where you might be hiding behind 'God's will' as an excuse for your own lack of readiness to change.
?
If God can change the camel and the needle to make the impossible possible, what does that mean for the 'impossible' habits, sins, or broken relationships in your life? Are you willing to ask God to change your receptivity — even if it means you must become small or the problem must become large?
