God's Hearing and Sight: Universality and Transcendence
Defines God's hearing and sight as universal, eternal attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that God's hearing and sight are eternal attributes that encompass every existent, and that they are fundamentally different from human hearing and sight.

Core Idea
God's hearing and sight disclose all existents; ours are limited.

This lesson begins with the poetic line commanding firm belief in God's hearing and sight. The commentary explains that these two attributes relate to every existing thing that is known to God, not just sounds or visible objects. We will see how they differ from our own sensory faculties, which are dependent on physical organs and conditions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on hearing and sight
Read the Arabic and translation of the first part: 'And be certain that His hearing and sight relate to every existent that is seen...'
Notice the word 'يُرى' is explained as 'known'.
2
Identify the key terms
Find and understand the terms 'سمع' (hearing) and 'بصر' (sight) as attributes of God.
They are eternal and subsisting in the essence.
3
Contrast with human faculties
Read the commentary comparing divine and human hearing/sight. Note the limitations of ours.
Human hearing depends on distance; human sight on direction and conditions.
4
Reflect on transcendence
Ask yourself: Why must God's hearing and sight be different from ours? What does that teach about His perfection?
This affirms divine transcendence (tanzih).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واجزم بأن سمعه والبصرا * تعلقا بكل موجود يرى 40 وكلها قديمة بالذات * لأنها ليست بغير الذات. (واجزم) أي: اعتقد اعتقادًا جازمًا أيها المكلف بأن سمعه تعالى والبصرا بألف الإطلاق تعلقا معًا تعلق انكشاف بكل موجود يرى، أي: يُعلم، أي: بكل موجود معلوم له تعالى. فهما صفتان قديمتان قائمتان بذاته تعالى فسمعه وبصره تعالى يخالفان سمعنا وبصرنا في التعلق، لأن سمعنا يتعلق عادة ببعض موجودات، وهي الأصوات، بشرط عدم البعد جدا، وبصرنا يتعلق عادة ببعض الموجودات، وهي الأجسام وألوانها في جهة مخصوصة، على وجه مخصوص. (وكلها) أي: كل صفات المعاني المتقدمة قديمة بالذات أي: صفاته تعالى قديمة كذاته بذات الله تعالى، فليست بممكنة، بل هي قديمة، وأن قدمها ذاتي بقدم الذات، واستدل على ذلك بقوله: ( لأنها ليست بغير الذات العلية (1) وأما سمعنا وبصرنا فحادثان قائمان بمحل مخصوص، فبصرنا قائم بإنسان العين أو هو قوة مودعة في العصبتين المجوفتين اللتين يتلاقيان ثم يفترقان، كما هو مذهب الحكماء، وسمعنا قائم بالصماخ - أي: ثقب الأذن أو هو قوة قائمة بالعصب المفروش في مقعر الصماخ، والله تعالى منزه عن ذلك، وسمعنا وبصرنا من أسباب علومنا، بخلاف سمعه وبصره تعالى.
And be certain that His hearing and sight relate to every existent that is seen (i.e., known). And all of them are eternal by essence because they are not other than the essence. (Wajzim) i.e., believe with firm belief, O responsible one, that His hearing, exalted, and sight – with the alif of absolute [pronunciation] – relate together a relation of disclosure to every existent that is seen, i.e., known, i.e., to every existent known to Him, exalted. So they are two eternal attributes subsisting in His essence, exalted. So His hearing and sight, exalted, differ from our hearing and sight in relation, because our hearing relates customarily to some existents, namely sounds, on condition of not being too distant, and our sight relates customarily to some existents, namely bodies and their colors, in a specific direction, in a specific manner. (And all of them) i.e., all the previous attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani) are eternal by essence, i.e., His attributes, exalted, are eternal like His essence, by the essence of God, exalted. So they are not possible, rather they are eternal, and their eternality is inherent by the eternality of the essence. And he proved that by his statement: 'Because they are not other than the lofty essence.' (1) As for our hearing and sight, they are originated, subsisting in a specific locus. Our sight subsists in the pupil of the eye, or it is a power deposited in the two hollow nerves that meet then separate, as is the doctrine of the philosophers. And our hearing subsists in the ear canal – i.e., the hole of the ear – or it is a power subsisting in the nerve spread in the concavity of the ear canal. And God, exalted, is transcendent of that. And our hearing and sight are among the causes of our knowledge, unlike His hearing and sight, exalted.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. It begins with the author's line: 'And be certain that His hearing and sight relate to every existent that is seen' – where 'seen' means known. The commentary explains: one must firmly believe that God's hearing (sam') and sight (basar) are two eternal attributes that disclose every existent known to Him. They differ from human hearing and sight. Human hearing relates to sounds not too distant; human sight relates to bodies and colors in a specific direction and manner. Then the author states: 'And all of them are eternal by essence because they are not other than the essence.' The commentary clarifies: 'all of them' refers to all the previously mentioned attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani). They are eternal like God's essence, not possible; their eternality is inherent from the eternality of the essence. The proof is: they are not other than the essence, so they share its eternal nature. A footnote adds: human hearing and sight are originated, subsisting in physical organs (pupil, nerves, ear canal) according to philosophers, and God is transcendent of that. Human hearing and sight cause knowledge, unlike God's.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking God's hearing and sight are like human sensory perception, limited to sounds and visible objects in a specific way.
Correction: God's hearing and sight encompass every existent (ma'lūm) without any limitation. They are not dependent on organs or physical conditions.

Checkpoint
What does the commentary say about the relation (tacalluq) of God's hearing and sight?
A) It is limited to sounds and colors.
B) It is a relation of disclosure to every existent known to Him.
C) It depends on physical distance.
D) It only applies to humans.

Key Takeaway
God's hearing and sight are eternal, universal attributes that disclose all existents, completely unlike human senses.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe God hears and sees every existent, including your most private thoughts and actions. Are you living with this awareness, or do you often act as if only your limited senses are watching? What specific habit would you need to change to truly honor that God sees and hears everything?
?
Your hearing and sight are confined to sounds at a distance and bodies in a direction. How does this contrast with God's unlimited perception humble you? In what area of your life do you need to submit your limited judgment to God's all-encompassing knowledge?
?
God's hearing and sight are eternal and unchanging—they do not fade or rest. Since you cannot escape His awareness, what sin are you still clinging to secretly, thinking it is hidden? Will you confess it to God and ask His forgiveness before you meet Him?

The Eternity of All Attributes of Meanings
States the eternity of all attributes of meanings and gives the proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that all attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani) are eternal by essence, and the proof is that they are not other than the essence.

Core Idea
Attributes are eternal because they are not separate from God's essence.

In the previous lesson we saw that hearing and sight are eternal. Now the author extends this to all attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani) – the seven attributes: life, knowledge, will, power, hearing, sight, speech. He states that each is eternal by essence, not possible. The proof is simple yet profound: they are not other than the essence.

Guided Path
1
Read the statement about all attributes
Read the line: 'And all of them are eternal by essence because they are not other than the essence.'
The phrase 'كلها' refers to all previously mentioned attributes of meanings.
2
Understand the proof
The proof is direct: if attributes are not other than the essence, they share the essence's eternal nature.
This avoids the need for a separate argument for each attribute.
3
Contrast with human attributes
Read the footnote about human hearing and sight being originated and physical.
This highlights that divine attributes are not like created ones.
4
Reflect on the implications
Why is it important that attributes are not other than the essence? What does this safeguard against?
It avoids multiplicity of eternals and affirms tawhid.

?
You affirm that God's attributes are eternal—but does your heart truly rest in that, or do you sometimes act as if God's attributes could change or fail? What would change in your trust if you fully believed His power and knowledge are co-eternal with Him?

The Eternality of Divine Speech: Beyond Letters and Sequence
Commentary on the matn line that Allah's speech is not composed of letters or sequence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah's speech is not composed of letters or sounds and does not involve temporal sequence, distinguishing it from created human speech.

Core Idea
Divine speech is an eternal attribute, not a sequence of letters or sounds.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies a crucial distinction: Allah's speech (kalam) is unlike human speech. While our speech is created, composed of letters and sounds, and unfolds in time, Allah's speech is an eternal attribute of His essence. This lesson explores the implications of that difference, based on the matn line that negates letters, sounds, and arrangement from divine speech. By understanding this, you will guard against anthropomorphic misconceptions about God's attribute of speech.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation. Notice the key terms: حرف (letter), صوت (sound), ترتيب (arrangement), حادث (temporal), قديمة (eternal).
The passage is a commentary on a matn line.
2
Identify what is negated
The passage explicitly negates that divine speech consists of letters, sounds, or temporal arrangement. Underline these negations in the text.
3
Grasp the contrast with human speech
The commentator contrasts divine speech with human speech, which is temporally originated (حادث) and consists of letters and sounds. Reflect on why this contrast is essential for tawheed.
4
Understand the positive affirmation
The passage affirms that divine speech is an attribute of His sacred essence (صفة ذاته المقدسة). This means speech is not an accident but a permanent attribute. How does this affect our understanding of Allah's communication?
5
Apply the lesson
Consider how this knowledge refutes the view that the Quran (as divine speech) is created. The Quran's eternity is defended by this distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كلامه تعالى ثم لما ذكر سابقا أن من صفات المعاني الكلام، أراد أن يبين أنه ليس ليس ككلام ككلامنا، فإن كلامنا حادث، وهو بحرف وصوت، وكلام الله عَزَّوَجَلَّ صفة قديمة فقال: (ثم الكلام ليس بالحروف والأصوات، (وليس) مُتَلَبّسًا (بالترتيب) والتأخير، (كـ) الكلام الحادث (المألوف) والمعروف لنا، فهو داخل في حيز النفي، أي أنه ليس مثل كلامنا، بل هو صفة ذاته المقدسة في الكلام، فالكلام لا ينحصر في الحروف والأصوات.
His speech, may He be exalted, then when he mentioned previously that among the attributes of meanings is speech, he intended to clarify that it is not like our speech, for our speech is temporally originated (hādith), and it consists of letters and sound, while the speech of Allah, the Mighty and Majestic, is an eternal attribute. So he said: (Then speech is not from letters and sounds, nor is it clothed with arrangement and delay, like the temporally originated familiar speech known to us), so it enters into the domain of negation, i.e., it is not like our speech, but rather it is an attribute of His sacred essence in terms of speech, so speech is not confined to letters and sounds.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn line regarding the attribute of speech (kalam). The author explains that after mentioning speech among the attributes of meanings, he wants to clarify that divine speech is not like human speech. Human speech is temporally originated (hādith) and consists of letters and sounds, while Allah's speech is an eternal attribute. The matn line negates that divine speech is composed of letters or sounds, and also negates that it involves arrangement or temporal sequence, as human speech does ('familiar speech'). Thus, divine speech is completely unlike created speech; it is an attribute of Allah's essence and is not limited to letters and sounds. The point is to affirm the eternity of divine speech and distinguish it from the contingent speech of creatures.

Attributes of Meaning: Additional and Inseparable
Explanation that divine attributes are not identical to essence and are inseparable.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the divine attributes of meaning are additional to the essence yet not distinct entities, and why this does not imply multiple eternal beings.

Core Idea
Divine attributes are real and inseparable, but not separate beings.

Sanusi addresses a subtle point in divine attribute theology: if attributes are real and eternal, do they become independent gods? This passage clarifies that while attributes are additional in meaning, they are not distinct essences. This careful distinction preserves tawhid while affirming God's perfect attributes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the author's concise argument style.
The passage contains a reductio ad absurdum and a response to an objection.
2
Identify the main claims
The passage makes three points: (1) attributes are inseparable from the essence, (2) they are not identical to the essence, and (3) this does not lead to multiple eternals. Find each claim.
3
Understand the objection and answer
Consider why someone might think attributes being additional implies multiple gods. Then see how Sanusi refutes this by denying distinction (taghāyur) between essence and attributes.
The key concept is 'taghāyur' – distinction in essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بمعنى أنها لا تنفك عنها، فلا يعقل قيام الذات بدونها، وليس وجودها في نفسها، وليست بعين الذات، وإلا لزم أن تكون الذات صفة، وأن الحياة عين العلم مثلا، وهو باطل. ولا يلزم من ذلك تعدد القدماء، لأن المحظور إنما هو تعدد القدماء المتغايرة، ونحن نمنع تغير الذات الصفات، والصفات بعضها مع بعض فينتفي التعدد، إذ لا يقبل إلا مع التغاير، فلا يلزم التعدد ولا التكثر ولا قدم الغير، ولا تكثر القدماء. قال العلامة السباعي أثر ذلك: «فعلِم أن مذهب أهل السنة: أن صفات الذات زائدة عليها، قائمة بها، لازمة لها لزوما لا يقبل الانفكاك فهي دائمة الوجود والبقاء، فلا يطرأ [عليها] العدم فهو حي بحياة عالم بعلم قادر بقدرة وهكذا».
Meaning that they [the attributes] are not separable from it [the essence]; thus the subsistence of the essence without them is inconceivable. Their existence is not in themselves, nor are they the same as the essence; otherwise it would necessitate that the essence be an attribute, and that life be identical to knowledge for example, which is false. And from that does not follow multiplicity of the Pre-eternals, because the forbidden thing is only multiplicity of distinct Pre-eternals, and we deny that the essence and the attributes, and the attributes with one another, are distinct; so multiplicity is negated, for it is only accepted with distinction. Thus multiplicity, numerousness, pre-eternality of another, and numerousness of Pre-eternals do not follow. Al-ʿAllāma al-Sibāʿī said following that: 'Know that the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna is that the attributes of the essence are additional to it, subsisting by it, inseparable from it with an inseparability that does not admit disconnection; so they are perpetual in existence and duration; non-existence does not occur to them. So He is living by a life, knowing by a knowledge, powerful by a power, and so forth.'
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage explains the nature of the divine attributes of meaning (ṣifāt al-maʿānī). The author asserts that these attributes are inseparable from the divine essence—it is inconceivable for the essence to exist without them. However, they are not existent in themselves (i.e., they are not independent substances) nor are they identical to the essence. If they were identical, that would imply that the essence is an attribute, which is absurd, or that life is the same as knowledge, which is also false. The author then addresses a possible objection: if the attributes are additional to the essence, does that entail multiple eternal beings? He answers no, because multiplicity requires distinct (mutaghāyira) entities. The attributes are not distinct from the essence or from each other in a way that would make them separate pre-eternals; they are distinct only in meaning (maʿna). Therefore, the fear of multiple eternals is unfounded. To reinforce this, he quotes al-Sibāʿī, who states the standard Sunni position: the attributes of the essence are additional (zāʾida) to the essence, subsisting by it, inseparable, perpetual, and never subject to non-existence. This is the madhhab of Ahl al-Sunna.

Key Terms
صفات الذات
زيادة
عدم الانفكاك
تعدد القدماء
التغاير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Saying the attributes are additional to the essence implies multiple eternal gods.
Correction: The attributes are not distinct essences; distinction in meaning alone does not produce multiplicity of eternals.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does saying attributes are additional not lead to multiple eternals?
A) Because attributes are identical to essence
B) Because attributes are not distinct essences (mutaghāyira)
C) Because attributes are created
D) Because attributes are independent

Key Takeaway
The Sunni position affirms that God's attributes are real, additional in meaning, inseparable, and eternal, yet they do not compromise the oneness of God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah as al-‘Alīm (the All-Knowing), do you truly feel that His knowledge is a real, eternal attribute inseparable from Him, or do you reduce it to a mere mental concept? How would your dua change if you internalized that His knowledge is as real as His existence?
?
The lesson argues that attributes are not identical to the essence yet not separate beings. Does this truth comfort you or challenge your mental image of God? Are you willing to accept a mystery that your mind cannot fully reconcile, or do you find yourself leaning toward simplification (denying the attributes) or exaggeration (making them gods)?

If you truly believed that Allah’s life, knowledge, and power are inseparable from Him and eternal, what would you change about your reliance on Him today? Identify one specific worry you have and ask yourself: is it because you forget that His power is not a distant concept but a present, inseparable reality?

Divine Speech Is Not Composed of Letters or Sounds
Affirmation that divine speech is a pre-eternal attribute not consisting of letters/sounds or temporal sequence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that divine speech is a pre-eternal attribute distinct from human speech, not composed of letters or sounds, and not subject to temporal sequence.

Core Idea
Allah's speech is an eternal attribute, not bound by letters, sounds, or time.

In this passage, the commentator builds on the earlier mention of speech as an attribute of meaning. He clarifies that divine speech is fundamentally different from human speech. While human speech is created, composed of letters and sounds, and occurs in time, Allah's speech is eternal and transcends these limitations. This distinction is crucial for avoiding anthropomorphic misconceptions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the negation pattern: 'ليس بالحروف وليس بالترتيب'.
2
Identify the key contrast
The passage contrasts divine speech with human speech. Find the specific features that are denied of divine speech (letters, sounds, arrangement).
Human speech is described as 'حادث' (originated) and 'بالحروف والأصوات'.
3
Understand the theological point
The passage affirms that divine speech is a 'صفة قديمة' (pre-eternal attribute) of the essence. Reflect on why this matters for tawhid.
This guards against anthropomorphism (tashbih).
4
Internalize the distinction
Summarize in your own words: How is Allah's speech different from ours? Why can't we think of it as composed of letters or sounds?
The key is that Allah's speech is not like any created thing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثُمَّ الكَلامُ لَيْس بالحُروفِ وَلَيْسَ بالترتيب كَالْمَأْلُونِ المخلوقين. كلامه تعالى ثم لما ذكر سابقا أن من صفات المعاني الكلام، أراد أن يبين أنه ليس ليس ككلام ككلامنا، فإن كلامنا حادث، وهو بحرف وصوت، وكلام الله عَزَّوَجَلَّ صفة قديمة فقال: (ثم الكلام ليس بالحروف والأصوات، (وليس) مُتَلَبّسًا (بالترتيب) والتأخير، (كـ) الكلام الحادث (المألوف) والمعروف لنا، فهو داخل في حيز النفي، أي أنه ليس مثل كلامنا، بل هو صفة ذاته المقدسة في الكلام، فالكلام لا ينحصر في الحروف والأصوات.
Then speech is not by letters nor by arrangement, like the customary [speech] of created beings. His speech—may He be exalted—then when he previously mentioned that among the attributes of meaning is speech, he intended to show that it is not like our speech; for our speech is originated, and it is by letter and sound, while the speech of Allah, mighty and majestic, is a pre-eternal attribute. So he said: 'Then speech is not by letters and sounds, nor is it attired by arrangement and delay, like the originated speech that is customary and known to us.' So it falls within the scope of negation, i.e., it is not like our speech; rather it is the attribute of His sacred essence in speech, for speech is not confined to letters and sounds.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage explains the nature of the divine attribute of speech (al-kalām). The author quotes the verse from al-Kharīda: 'Then speech is not by letters nor by arrangement, like the customary [speech] of created beings.' He then interprets it, explaining that speech is one of the attributes of meaning, and the author aims to distinguish it from human speech. Human speech is originated (ḥādith), composed of letters and sounds, and occurs in temporal sequence. In contrast, Allah's speech is a pre-eternal (qadīm) attribute that is not composed of letters or sounds, nor does it involve temporal arrangement or delay. He emphasizes that divine speech is not like our speech; it is an attribute of the essence, not confined to letters and sounds. The key point is that speech in God is a real attribute, but it is not the speech of created beings—it is transcendent, not limited by physical or temporal properties.

Proof That the Opposites of Allah's Attributes Are Impossible
Mashāṭ provides a logical proof by conditional that the opposites of Allah's attributes are impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical proof that the opposites of Allah's attributes are impossible, based on the entailment of poverty and temporality.

Core Idea
If Allah lacked a perfection, He would have its opposite, which implies need — impossible for the Self-Sufficient.

Mashāṭ presents a concise proof that the opposites of Allah’s attributes cannot exist. The reasoning is conditional: if Allah were not perfect, He would be imperfect; imperfection implies need and contingency, which contradicts His necessary existence. This lesson unpacks that argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage consists of three poem lines followed by a commentary that explains the logic.
2
Follow the conditional argument
Identify the conditional premise: if Allah were not described with lofty attributes, He would be described with their opposites. Notice how the commentary explains that this leads to poverty and temporality, which are impossible.
The proof is not circular; it uses the fact that absence of a perfection implies presence of its opposite.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that because Allah is self-sufficient and omnipotent, He cannot be characterized by any deficiency. Thus, the opposites of His attributes are impossible.
This reinforces the doctrine of divine perfection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٤٤ | لأَنَّهُ لَوْ لَمْ يَكُنْ مَوْصُوفا * بِهَا لَكَانَ بِالسِّوَى مَعْرُوفا ٤٥ وكُلُّ مَنْ قَامَ بِهِ سِوَاهَا * فَهْوَ الذِي فِي الفَقْرِ قَدْ تَنَاهَى ٤٦ والوَاحِدُ الْمَعْبُودُ لا يَفْتَقِرُ * لِغَيْرِهِ جَلَّ الغَنِي الْمُقْتَدِرُ شرح الشيخ حسن محمد المشاط الملكي المالكي . وإنما وجبت له الصفات العُلى واستحال عليه أضدادها: (لأنه) تَبَارَكَ وَتَعَالَى (لو لم يكن موصوفًا بها لكان بالسوى) بكسر السين، وفتح الواو مقصورًا، والألف واللام عوض عن المضاف إليه، ويتعلق بقوله: (معروفًا) أي: لكان معروفًا بسواها، في العجز والجهل إلى آخر المستحيلات، يعني: لو لم يكن متصفًا بالصفات الشامخات لا تصف بأضدادها، لكن اتصافه بأضدادها ،باطل لما يلزم عليه من الافتقار والحدوث، كما قال الناظم رحمه الله تعالى: وكل من قام به سواها فاعل ،قام أي كل من قام به غير الصفات العلى الشامخات من العجز والجهل وغيرهما من المستحيلات، فهو الذي في الفقر والاحتياج إلى من يكمله (قد تناهي) أي: قد تناهى في الفقر والاحتياج، فقوله: «في الفقر يتعلق بقوله: «قد تناهى»، وهو محال، لأنه يؤدي إلى الحدوث. والواو في قول الناظم: ( والواحد) لعلها للاستئناف، أو للحال، أي: والحال أن الله تعالى الواحد الذي هو (المعبود) بحق (لا يفتقر لغيره) بل غيره مفتقر إليه تعالى (جل) وعظم عن ذلك الافتقار الله (الغني) بسكون الياء للوزن أي: الغني عن كل ما سواه، (المقتدر) على كل شيء، وكل شيء هو إلى الغني المقتدر فقير.
44. 'Because if He were not described with them, He would be known by their opposite.' 45. 'And whoever is characterized by other than them, he is the one who has reached the extreme in poverty.' 46. 'And the One, the rightly worshiped, is not in need of others; majestic is the Self-Sufficient, the Omnipotent.' Commentary by Sheikh Hasan Muhammad al-Mishāt al-Mālikī. And the lofty attributes are obligatory for Him and their opposites are impossible for Him: because He, blessed and exalted, if He were not described with them, He would be described by what is other than them using kasra on the sīn and fatḥa on the wāw (al-siwā), alif and lām compensating for the omitted possessive, and it is related to His saying: 'known' — i.e., He would be known by other than them, i.e., by impotence, ignorance, and other impossibilities. That is, if He were not characterized by the lofty attributes, He would be characterized by their opposites. But His characterization by their opposites is invalid because it entails poverty (iftaqār) and temporality (ḥudūth), as the author said: 'And whoever is characterized by other than them' — i.e., whoever is characterized by other than the lofty attributes such as impotence, ignorance, and other impossibilities, then he is the one who has reached the extreme in poverty and need to one who completes him. His saying 'in poverty' is related to his saying 'has reached the extreme' — and that is impossible because it leads to temporality. The wāw in the author's saying: 'and the One' is perhaps for resumption or for the circumstantial state, i.e., the state being that Allah, the One who is rightly worshiped, is not in need of others; rather, others are in need of Him. He is exalted and glorified above that poverty. Allah is the Self-Sufficient (with sukūn on the yā' for meter), i.e., independent of all else, and the Omnipotent over everything, and everything is poor in need of the Self-Sufficient, the Omnipotent.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage provides the logical proof (burhān) that the opposites of the necessary attributes are impossible for Allah. It is a classification of an argument. The proof is structured as a conditional: if Allah were not described with the lofty attributes (e.g., life, knowledge, power), then He would be described by their opposites (ignorance, impotence, etc.). But being described by those opposites entails poverty (iftaqār) and temporality (ḥudūth), which are impossible for Allah. The poem lines reinforce this: line 44 states the conditional premise, line 45 notes that anyone characterized by deficiencies is in extreme poverty, and line 46 declares that the One God is not in need. The commentator explains that the 'poverty' here means neediness for a perfecter, and this implies contingency, which is impossible for Allah. The wāw in 'and the One' is interpreted as resumptive or circumstantial, emphasizing that Allah is self-sufficient and all others are dependent on Him. The proof does not assume the conclusion; it shows that if Allah lacked a perfection, He would have its opposite, which is a deficiency, but deficiency is impossible for the necessarily existent being.

Key Terms
وجوب
استحالة
صفات عُلى
افتقار
حدوث
غني
مقتدر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof is circular: it assumes Allah must have perfect attributes.
Correction: The proof does not assume the conclusion; it uses the principle that absence of a perfection entails its opposite, which implies need and temporality. Since need is impossible for Allah, the conclusion follows logically.

Checkpoint
According to the proof presented, what would follow if Allah were not described with the lofty attributes?
A) He would be described with their opposites, which entail poverty and temporality.
B) He would be described with even more perfect attributes.
C) He would be unknown and unknowable.
D) He would have no attributes at all.

Key Takeaway
The proof shows that any imperfection implies need, which is impossible for Allah, so He must possess all perfections and their opposites cannot exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believed that any deficiency in Allah is impossible because it would entail need, would you ever dare to doubt His knowledge of your hidden acts? How does this lesson confront your secret sins?
?
The proof shows that you are the one in need, not Allah. Are there areas of your life where you act as if Allah depends on your worship or obedience? How can you shift your mindset to worship out of your own need rather than duty?
?
If Allah's opposite attributes are impossible, then His promise of reward and threat of punishment are certain. Is there a command you have been neglecting that this certainty now makes urgent? What will you do about it today?

Why God Must Have Exalted Attributes
Commentary explains why God must have attributes and cannot have opposites.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why God must be described with exalted attributes and why their opposites are impossible, and how this relates to God's independence and omnipotence.

Core Idea
God's attributes are necessary; their opposites would imply need and temporality.

In this passage, the commentator argues for the necessity of God's attributes by considering the alternative: if God were not described with His exalted attributes, He would be characterized by their opposites, such as impotence and ignorance. But such opposites are impossible because they would imply need and temporal origination, which are incompatible with divinity. The passage then affirms God's absolute self-sufficiency and omnipotence.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Start by reading the Arabic passage carefully. Notice the conditional structure starting with 'لو لم يكن' and how the proof unfolds. Then read the English translation to ensure understanding.
The passage uses a conditional argument and a poetic citation.
2
Understand the core argument
The commentator states that if God were not described with exalted attributes, He would be known by something else—ultimately their opposites. This is the starting point of the argument.
3
Examine the proof
The proof is that opposite attributes (like impotence) would entail need (iftiqār) and temporal origination (ḥudūth), both impossible for God. Trace why need implies temporality.
The poet's line reinforces this: anyone with other than these attributes is in utter poverty.
4
Conclude with God's sufficiency
The passage ends by affirming that God is the One, the Self-Sufficient (al-Ghanī), and the Omnipotent (al-Muqtadir), and everything else is in need of Him.
This resolves the argument: God cannot have opposites because He is inherently perfect and independent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإنما وجبت له الصفات العلى واستحال عليه أضدادها: (لأنه) تبارك وتعالى (لو لم يكن موصوفًا بها لكان بالسوى) بكسر السين، وفتح الواو مقصورًا، والألف واللام عوض عن المضاف إليه، ويتعلق بقوله: (معروفًا) أي: لكان معروفًا بسواها، في العجز والجهل إلى آخر المستحيلات، يعني: لو لم يكن متصفًا بالصفات الشامخات لاتصف بأضدادها، لكن اتصافه بأضدادها باطل لما يلزم عليه من الافتقار والحدوث، كما قال الناظم رحمه الله تعالى: وكل من قام به سواها فاعل ،قام أي كل من قام به غير الصفات العلى الشامخات من العجز والجهل وغيرهما من المستحيلات، فهو الذي في الفقر والاحتياج إلى من يكمله (قد تناهي) أي: قد تناهى في الفقر والاحتياج، فقوله: «في الفقر» يتعلق بقوله: «قد تناهى»، وهو محال، لأنه يؤدي إلى الحدوث. والواو في قول الناظم: ( والواحد) لعلها للاستئناف، أو للحال، أي: والحال أن الله تعالى الواحد الذي هو (المعبود) بحق (لا يفتقر لغيره) بل غيره مفتقر إليه تعالى (جل) وعظم عن ذلك الافتقار الله (الغني) بسكون الياء للوزن أي: الغني عن كل ما سواه، (المقتدر) على كل شيء، وكل شيء هو إلى الغني المقتدر فقير.
And indeed the high attributes are necessary for Him, and their opposites are impossible for Him: (because) He, blessed and exalted, (if He were not described with them, He would be known by something else) —with kasra on the sin, and fatha on the waw (the word as-suwa), and the alif and lam are in place of the possessive construction, and it is attached to His statement: (known)—i.e., He would be known by something other than these attributes, in impotence and ignorance and other impossibilities. Meaning: if He were not characterized by the lofty attributes, He would be characterized by their opposites, but His characterization by their opposites is false because of what would follow from that of need (iftiqār) and temporal origination (ḥudūth), as the versifier, may Allah have mercy on him, said: 'And whoever is characterized by something other than them, he is the one who has reached the utmost in poverty.' So he said: 'whoever is characterized by other than the high, lofty attributes—such as impotence, ignorance, and other impossibilities—then he is the one in poverty and need of one who completes him; (has reached the utmost) i.e., he has reached the utmost in poverty and need. So his saying: 'in poverty' is attached to his saying: 'has reached the utmost', and that is impossible, because it leads to temporal origination. And the wāw in the versifier's saying: 'and the One' is perhaps for resumption, or for the circumstantial clause, i.e., while the case is that Allah, the One who is (truly worshipped) (does not need others), rather others are in need of Him, exalted. He is far above that need. (al-Ghanī) with sukun on the yā' for meter, i.e., the Self-Sufficient from everything other than Him, (al-Muqtadir) over everything, and everything is poor (in need) towards the Self-Sufficient, the Omnipotent.
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage is a theological argument establishing the necessity of God's attributes and the impossibility of their opposites. The commentator explains the verse 'And indeed the high attributes are necessary for Him...' by stating a conditional: if God were not described with these attributes, He would be known by something else—namely their opposites like impotence and ignorance, which are impossible. The proof is that attributing opposites to God would imply need (iftiqār) and temporal origination (ḥudūth), both impossible for the Creator. The commentator then cites the poet's line that anyone characterized by other than these lofty attributes (i.e., by imperfections) is in utter poverty and need of a perfecter, which leads to temporality. Finally, the commentator discusses the word 'al-wāḥid' (the One), explaining that God is the truly worshipped who does not need others; rather, all others depend on Him. He emphasizes that God is al-Ghanī (the Self-Sufficient) and al-Muqtadir (the Omnipotent), and everything is poor towards Him.

Correction: The passage shows that God's attributes are necessary concomitants of His essence; lacking them would imply opposites, which would entail need and temporality, impossible for God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The passage says God is Self-Sufficient and everything else is poor toward Him. If you truly believed that you are utterly dependent on God in every moment, how would your attitude toward your own accomplishments change? What would you stop boasting about? This week, when you feel proud, remind yourself that you are the poor one in need. Can you commit to a daily pause where you say 'al-Ghaniyy' and meditate on your need?
?
If you were not described with the lofty attributes, you would be described with their opposites. That is a logical truth. But do you live as if God's attributes are real and necessary? Or do you treat His attributes as mere names you recite without conviction? The lesson tells you that God's attributes are necessary for His perfection. Does your heart tremble when you hear that God is all-powerful and all-knowing, or do you reduce Him to a concept? Resolve to whisper 'al-Muqtadir' and feel its weight tonight.

The Permissible in God's Right: Creation and Leaving
Author introduces third division (al-ja'iz) for God and gives examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of 'jā'iz' (permissible) in relation to God and recognize examples of permissible divine actions.

Core Idea
God’s actions are never obligated; creation and leaving are equally permissible.

In Islamic theology, actions are divided into necessary, impossible, and permissible for God. This lesson focuses on the permissible (jā'iz)—actions that God may or may not do, with no constraint. The author provides key examples to clarify this concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Let the author's own words set the stage before analysis.
Notice that the passage defines jā'iz and then lists examples.
2
Identify the definition of jā'iz
Find the sentence that explains what jā'iz means regarding God's actions. Understand that it means God may do or not do something.
3
Analyze the examples given
List the examples: sending messengers, rewarding the disobedient, punishing the obedient, and creating disbelief. Notice how each is presented as not obligatory on God.
Pay attention to the term 'ishqā' and its synonyms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم شرع الناظم رحمه الله تعالى في بيان القسم الثالث، وهو الجائز، فقال: (وجائز في حقه تعالى — (الإيجاد)، والترك) بالرفع عطف على «الإيجاد»، أي: يجوز في حقه تعالى إيجاد الممكنات وتركها يعني أن إيجاد كل ممكن أو تركه أمر جائز في حقه تعالى، إن شاء فعل، وإن شاء لم يفعله. قال المؤلف: ومن ذلك : بعثة الرسل عليهم الصلاة والسلام، فإن بعثتهم جائزة وليست بواجبة على الله تعالى، وكذلك إثابة العاصي، وتعذيب المطيع. والإشقاء) وهو خلق الكفر في العبد والعياذ بالله تعالى، ويسمى الخذلان - بكسر الخاء، والإضلال.
Then the versifier, may Allah have mercy on him, began to explain the third division, which is the permissible (al-jā'iz), and said: (And permissible in His right, exalted, is— (origination [ījād]), and leaving [tark]) —with raf' as a conjunction to 'origination', i.e., it is permissible in His right, exalted, to originate contingent things and to leave them, meaning that the origination of every contingent thing or its leaving is a permissible matter in His right, exalted; if He wills, He does it, and if He wills, He does not do it. The author said: Among that is the sending of the messengers, peace and blessings be upon them, for their sending is permissible and not obligatory upon Allah, exalted; likewise, rewarding the disobedient and punishing the obedient. And (making wretched [al-ishqā']) which is creating disbelief in the servant, and refuge is sought with Allah from that, and it is called al-khidhlān (with kasr on the khā') and al-iḍlāl (misguidance).
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage defines and illustrates the third category of what is possible (jā'iz) with respect to God. The commentator explains that the poet begins by stating 'and permissible in His right is origination and leaving.' The term 'permissible' here means that it is equally possible for God to create or not create any contingent thing; it is not necessary nor impossible. The commentator clarifies that origination (ījād) and leaving (tark) are both permissible. Then he provides examples: the sending of messengers is permissible, not obligatory upon God; similarly, rewarding a disobedient person or punishing an obedient one is permissible. Finally, he explains 'making wretched' (ishqā') as the creation of disbelief in a servant, which is also among the permissible acts of God, and it is called khidhlān (abandonment) and iḍlāl (leading astray).

الإيجاد
الترك
الإشقاء
الخذلان
الإضلال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God is obligated to send messengers or to reward obedient people.
Correction: The passage shows these actions are purely permissible (jā'iz) and not obligatory upon God.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT given as an example of the permissible (jā'iz) in God's right?
A) Creating disbelief in a servant
B) Punishing an obedient person
C) Requiring worship from humans
D) Sending messengers

Key Takeaway
The permissible (jā'iz) for God means that He may act or refrain, and examples include creating, leaving, sending messengers, and creating disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Rewarding the disobedient and punishing the obedient are permissible in God's right. How does this truth affect your reliance on your own good deeds? Are you truly hoping for His mercy, not your merit?
?
God may create disbelief in a servant. Does this make you fear His justice and rely solely on His grace? How does this shape your dua for steadfastness?

Refutation of Obligatory Goodness upon Allah
The poem asserts that it is not obligatory for Allah to do good, refuting the Mu'tazila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Ahl al-Sunna reject the Mu'tazila claim that doing good is obligatory upon Allah, and why such a claim is considered ill-mannered.

Core Idea
Allah is not obligated to do anything; obligation violates His absolute freedom.

This lesson tackles a key difference between Ahl al-Sunna and the Mu'tazila. The Mu'tazila argued that Allah must do what is good and better for His creatures, but the Sunni position is that Allah's actions are not subject to any external obligation. The poem refutes this with a strong expression, stating that whoever claims obligation on Allah is ill-mannered.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the literal translation. Notice the strong language used in the poem.
2
Identify the two opposing views
The passage contrasts the Mu'tazila view (obligation) with the Sunni view (permissible). Understand the core disagreement.
3
Reflect on the concept of 'ill-mannered'
The poem says that claiming obligation on Allah is 'asa'a al-adab' (ill-mannered). Think why this would be disrespectful.
It implies that Allah is subject to external constraints, which contradicts His absolute sovereignty.
4
Connect to broader principle
Relate this to the Sunni doctrine that Allah is free in His actions and not bound by any external obligation. This is part of divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودخل في الجائز أيضًا فعل الصلاح والأصلح، وهو مذهب أهل السنة والجماعة، وأراد الناظم بيان ذلك والرد على مُقابله فقال: (ومن يقل : فعل الصلاح وجبا بألف الإطلاق، على الإله) على المعتزلة. وهم المعتزلة قبحهم الله.
And also included in the permissible is the action of what is good and better (al-salah wa al-aslah), and this is the doctrine of Ahl al-Sunna wa al-Jama'a. The author intended to clarify that and to refute its opponent, so he said: (And whoever says: The doing of good is obligatory upon Allah, has surely been ill-mannered.) Refuting the Mu'tazila. And they are the Mu'tazila, may Allah curse them.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage introduces the issue of al-salah wa al-aslah (the good and the better). The Mu'tazila held that it is obligatory upon Allah to do what is good and better for His creatures. The author of the poem refutes this, stating that such a claim is ill-mannered (asa'a al-adab). This is a key point of contention between Ahl al-Sunna and the Mu'tazila, affirming that Allah's actions are not subject to external obligation.

The Ash'ari-Maturidi Disagreement on Attributes of Action
Comparing Ash'ari and Maturidi on attributes of action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key differences between Ash'ari and Maturidi views on the attributes of action, including the Maturidi concept of takwin as an eternal attribute.

Core Idea
Ash'aris see attributes of action as created relations; Maturidis see them as a single eternal attribute called takwin.

The attributes of action (sifat al-af'al) are a point of doctrinal divergence between the Ash'ari and Maturidi schools. This lesson clarifies their respective positions, focusing on the Maturidi notion of takwin as an eternal attribute from which all action attributes derive, and how it differs from power (qudra). Read the passage and commentary to grasp the nuances.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the technical terms and the structure of the comparison.
The passage begins with the Maturidi position and then contrasts it with the Ash'ari view.
2
Identify the two schools' positions
Find where each school's view is stated: Ash'ari (created/haditha) and Maturidi (eternal/qadima). Note the basis for each.
Ash'aris see them as relations; Maturidis see them as a single eternal attribute.
3
Understand the concept of takwin
Takwin is introduced as a single attribute subsisting in God's essence, by which things exist. It is compared to power and will.
Takwin is the actual bringing into existence, while power is just the ability.
4
Distinguish between qudra and takwin
The passage explicitly differentiates them: qudra entails soundness of affecting, takwin entails existence. Also note that takwin is additional to both qudra and irada.
Power enables, will specifies, takwin actualizes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جميع ما نُعبّر عنه بصفات الأفعال جزم الماتريدية بقدمها، ومجموعها عند محققيهم عبارة عن صفةٍ واحدةٍ تسمى بالتكوين قائمة بذاته تعالى، لكونها صفة معنى، كالقدرة والإرادة، يتأتى بها وجود الأشياء على وفق الإرادة. والفرق بينها وبين القدرة: أن القدرة عندهم بها صحة التأثير في الممكن، والتكوين به وجود الأشياء. وجملة القول في ذلك أن الإيجاد والخلق والرزق، والإحياء والإماتة والإشقاء، والإسعاد، والتصوير إلى غير ذلك عند الأشعرية صفات حادثة، لأنها إضافات واعتبارات بين القدرة والمقدور. وعند الماتريدية قديمة، لأنها صفة أزلية بها صدور العالم، وكل جزء من أجزائه، وتسمى: تكوينا، لكن إن تعلقت بوجود الشيء سميت إيجادًا، وخلقا، أو بموته سميت إماتة، أو بصورته سميت: تصويرا، وهي زائدة على القدرة والإرادة، فإن الإرادة بها التخصيص، والقدرة هي القوة على فعل الشيء أو تركه، ونسبة الأمرين إليها على السواء، فليس بها صدور الأشياء، وإنما بها قبول الصدور ، فهي مبدأ القبول الصدور ، والتكوين مبدأ لنفس الصدور.
All that we express as attributes of actions, the Maturidis firmly believe they are eternal. The totality of them according to their verified scholars is a single attribute called takwin, subsisting in His essence, being an attribute of meaning, like power and will, by which the existence of things comes about in accordance with will. The difference between it and power is that power, according to them, entails the soundness of affecting the possible, while takwin entails the existence of things. The summary of the statement about that is: al-ijad, al-khalq, al-rizq, al-ihya', al-imata, al-ishqa', al-is'ad, al-taswir and so on, according to the Ash'aris are created attributes, because they are relations and considerations between power and the acted-upon. According to the Maturidis, they are eternal, because they are an eternal attribute by which the universe emanates, and every part of it, and it is called takwin. But if it attaches to the existence of something, it is called ijad or khalq; or to its death, it is called imata; or to its form, it is called taswir. And it is additional to power and will, for will entails specification, and power is the ability to do or leave an action, and the relation of both to it is equal. So by it (power) the emanation of things does not occur; rather, it is the means of accepting emanation. So it is the principle of accepting emanation, while takwin is the principle of the emanation itself.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This footnote compares the Ash'ari and Maturidi positions on the attributes of action (sifat al-af'al). The Ash'aris consider them created (haditha) because they are relations attached to power. The Maturidis consider them eternal (qadima) because they reduce to a single eternal attribute called takwin (bringing into existence). The passage explains the difference between power (qudra) and takwin: power is the ability to act, while takwin is the actual bringing into existence. It also notes that takwin is additional to power and will. This is a classic point of divergence between the two schools.

Key Terms
صفات الأفعال
تكوين
قدرة
إرادة
حادث
قديم
أشعرية
ماتريدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All attributes of action are one and the same in both schools.
Correction: Ash'aris see them as multiple created relations, while Maturidis see them as aspects of a single eternal attribute takwin.

Checkpoint
According to the Maturidis, what is the single eternal attribute from which all attributes of action derive?
A) Power (qudra)
B) Will (irada)
C) Takwin
D) Creation (khalq)

Key Takeaway
The Maturidis teach that all attributes of action reduce to a single eternal attribute, takwin, while the Ash'aris view them as multiple created relations attached to divine power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about God's actions in your life—like provision, life, and death—do you view them as separate, moment-by-moment creations, or as expressions of a single, eternal act of His? Which perspective gives you more certainty in His consistent care for you?
?
The Maturidis say God's power only makes emanation possible, but takwin actually brings things into existence. In your own spiritual life, do you rely more on your sense of God's ability to act or on His actual, constant bringing about of events? Is there an area where you need to trust that His takwin is at work right now?
?
This lesson shows deep theological disagreement among Muslims on the nature of God's attributes. Does it unsettle you that scholars differ, or does it deepen your humility before the unfathomable reality of God? How can you hold your own view with conviction while respecting other legitimate positions?

The Vision of Allah: Hereafter and This World
Commentary on the beatific vision: its certainty in paradise and its occurrence in dreams and for the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Islamic position on seeing Allah: its certainty in the afterlife, its impossibility while awake in this world, its possibility in dreams for the righteous, and its occurrence for the Prophet on the night of Isra.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah is certain in Paradise, possible in dreams, and occurred for the Prophet, but not while awake in this world.

This lesson examines the doctrinal points on the vision of Allah (ru'ya). The poem affirms its certainty in the eternal paradise. The commentary then clarifies its status in this world: it does not occur while awake, but is possible in dreams, as reported for many righteous including Imam Ahmad. A special case is the Prophet's seeing his Lord with his eyes on the night of Isra.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and the literal translation. Note the key terms.
2
Understand the three divisions
The passage divides the topic into: seeing in paradise (certain), seeing in this world awake (impossible), seeing in dreams (possible for some), and the Prophet's seeing (occurred with eyes).
3
Reflect on the Prophet's seeing
The commentator affirms the correct position that the Prophet saw Allah with his physical eyes, not just with his heart, on the night of Isra. Consider why this matters for theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاجْزِمُ أَخِي بِرُؤيَةِ الإِلهِ * فِي جَنَّةِ الْخُلْدِ بِلا تَنَاهِي. أَمَّا رُؤْيَتُهُ تَعَالَى فِي الدُّنْيَا يَقَظَةً فَغَيْرُ وَاقِعَةٍ، أَمَّا فِي الْمَنَامِ فَقَدْ وَقَعَتْ لِكَثِيرٍ مِنَ الصَّالِحِينَ مِنْهُمُ الْإِمَامُ أَحْمَدُ بْنُ حَنْبَلٍ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى، فَقَدْ نُقِلَ عَنْهُ إِنَّهُ رَأَى رَبَّهُ عَزَّ وَجَلَّ مِرَارًا كَثِيرَةً فِي الْمَنَامِ. وَالصَّحِيحُ أَنَّ نَبِيَّنَا صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ رَأَى رَبَّهُ بِالْبَصَرِ لَيْلَةَ الْإِسْرَاءِ لَا بِالْقَلْبِ فَقَطْ، وَاللَّهُ أَعْلَمُ.
And be certain, my brother, of seeing the God in the eternal paradise without end. As for seeing Him, Exalted, in this world while awake, it does not occur. As for in sleep, it has occurred for many righteous, among them Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal, may Allah have mercy on him, for it is reported that he saw his Lord, Mighty and Majestic, many times in sleep. And the correct view is that our Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, saw his Lord with his eyesight on the night of Isra, not only with the heart. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line from the poem affirming the certainty of seeing Allah in the eternal paradise. The commentator then addresses the topic of seeing Allah in this world. He states that seeing Allah while awake in this world does not occur (ghayr waqi'a). However, seeing Him in dreams has occurred for many righteous individuals, including Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal, who reportedly saw his Lord many times in dreams. Then he gives the correct position regarding the Prophet: on the night of the Isra, he saw his Lord with his physical eyesight (bil-basar), not just with the heart. The commentator ends with 'Wallahu a'lam' (Allah knows best).

God's Acts are Between Justice and Favor
Commentator explains divine acts as only justice or favor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that all divine acts are categorized as either justice or favor, and that reward is purely by grace, not merit.

Core Idea
God's justice is exact, but His favor is the source of all good.

In this passage, the commentator explains the principle that God's dealings with humanity are based on either justice or favor. He uses the examples of the punishment of the disobedient and the reward of the believers to illustrate this fundamental division. A quote from al-Laqani's famous poem 'Jawhara' reinforces the idea, and a gnostic saying warns that strict justice would leave no good deed standing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping, then read the literal translation. Notice the clear division between justice and favor.
The passage is divided into a statement, a quoted verse, and a gnostic saying.
2
Identify the two categories
Find the terms 'عدل' (justice) and 'فضل' (favor) in the passage. Notice which acts correspond to each: punishment of sinners is justice, reward of believers is favor.
The passage says these two categories cover all divine acts.
3
Reflect on the meaning of 'pure'
The commentator describes both as 'عين' (very essence) – punishment is pure justice, reward is pure favor. Think about why reward cannot be justice.
This highlights that humans do not deserve paradise by their deeds.
4
Consider the gnostic saying
The saying warns that if God applied strict justice, no good deed would remain. This underscores that justice alone would condemn everyone, and only favor saves.
This connects to the broader Islamic concept of mercy outweighing wrath.

Commentary
This passage is a theological note on the nature of God's actions. It categorizes all divine acts as either justice (adl) or favor (fadl). Specifically, punishing the disobedient is pure justice, while rewarding believers is pure favor. The commentator quotes al-Laqani's Jawhara in verse form to emphasize that any reward from God is by favor, any punishment by justice. He then cites a gnostic saying that if God applies strict justice, no good deed would remain; the implication is that God's mercy outweighs His justice. The text is cut off, but the point is clear: God's treatment of His servants is based on justice and favor, not obligation.

Divine Actions Are Between Justice and Favor
The author explains that God's actions are categorized as either justice or favor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that all divine actions are categorized as either justice (ʿadl) or favor (faḍl), and that reward is by pure favor while punishment is pure justice.

Core Idea
God's actions are never unjust; reward is grace, punishment is justice.

This lesson explores a foundational principle in Islamic theology: that nothing God does is arbitrary or unjust, yet He is not bound by human notions of fairness. Instead, His actions are always either justice or favor. We will see how this distinction explains why believers enter Paradise by grace and sinners enter Hell by justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify key terms
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the terms 'عدل' (justice) and 'فضل' (favor) which are the central concepts. Pay attention to how the author uses them in examples.
The passage uses poetry and prose to emphasize the point.
2
Examine the examples of justice and favor
The author gives two clear examples: punishing the disobedient is pure justice, and admitting the believers into Paradise is pure favor. Understand that these are not reciprocal obligations.
Justice and favor are not opposites; both are good actions of God.
3
Understand the implications from the gnostic saying
The gnostic saying illustrates the extreme effects: if God applies justice, no good deed remains; if He applies favor, no sin remains. This shows the absolute sufficiency of God's actions.
This highlights human dependence on God's grace.
4
Reflect on the poetry reinforcing divine sovereignty
The commentary poetry reiterates that God gives and withholds as He wills, without being influenced by any bribe or human effort. This reinforces His complete freedom and generosity.
The poetry is a mnemonic aid for the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن أفعال الله تعالى تدور بين العدل والفضل، حتى إن إدخال العصاة النار هو عين العدل، كما إن إدخال المؤمنين الجنة هو عين الفضل، قال العلامة الشيخ إبراهيم اللقاني في «جوهرته»: فإن يُثبنا فَبِمَحْضِ الفَضْل وإِنْ يُعَذِّب فَبِمَحْضِ العَدْلِ قال بعض العارفين: «إذا وضع عدله على عبد لم تبق له حسنة، وإذا بسط فضله على عبد لم تبق له سيئة. يُعطي ويمنع ما يشاء كما يَشَا * وَهِبَاتُه ليستْ تُقَارِنُهَا الرَّشَا يقول العبد الفقير غفرا الله له وختم له بخير آمين: إنْ وَضَعَ الجَلِيلُ عَدْلَهُ عَلَى عَبْدِ تَلاشَتْ حَسَنَاتِه الأُلَى وإِنْ يَكُنْ قَد بَسَطَ الفَضْلَ عَلَيْهِ لَمْ يَبْقَ مِنْ سَيِّئَةِ العَبْدِ لَدَيْهِ سُبْحَانَ رَبِّي ذِي عَطَاءٍ وَكَرَمْ * مَا شَاءَ أَوْجَدَ وَمَا شَاءَ عَدَمْ
Know that the actions of Allah Almighty revolve between justice and favor, so much so that admitting the disobedient into the Fire is the very essence of justice, just as admitting the believers into Paradise is the very essence of favor. The scholar Sheikh Ibrahim al-Laqani said in his Jawhara: 'If He rewards us, it is by pure favor; if He punishes, it is by pure justice.' Some gnostics said: 'When He places His justice upon a servant, no good deed remains for him; and when He spreads His favor upon a servant, no evil deed remains for him. He gives and withholds as He wills and as He wishes. His gifts are not accompanied by bribes.' The poor servant, may Allah forgive him and seal his end with good, says: 'If the Majestic places His justice on a servant, his former good deeds vanish; and if He spreads His favor upon him, none of the servant's evil deeds remain. Glory be to my Lord, full of giving and generosity. What He wills He brings into existence, and what He wills He annihilates.'
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is an exposition of the principle that all divine actions fall under two categories: justice (ʿadl) and favor (faḍl). The author begins by stating this general rule and then gives examples: punishing sinners is pure justice, and rewarding believers is pure favor. He then quotes Imam Ibrahim al-Laqani's lines from the Jawhara to reinforce this point. Next, he cites a gnostic saying that illustrates the implications: if God applies justice, a person's good deeds vanish (because they are insufficient for salvation without grace); if He applies favor, all sins are erased. The commentator adds his own poetic lines echoing the same theme, emphasizing God's absolute sovereignty and generosity. The key takeaway is that God is not obligated to reward or punish; His actions are based on His wisdom and will.

فضل
جوهرة
إبراهيم اللقاني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God is obligated to reward good deeds or that punishment is always a sign of God's injustice.
Correction: The passage clarifies that reward is pure favor (not owed) and punishment is pure justice (not unjust).

Checkpoint

A) It is injustice
B) It is pure justice
C) It is favor
D) It is necessary

Key Takeaway
God's actions are always just or gracious; reward is a gift, not a right.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The gnostic saying declares that when Allah spreads His favor, no sin remains. Are you truly living in the hope of that favor, or do you still let past sins haunt you as if His mercy is not enough?
?

Belief in Specific Angels and Rulings on Denial
Enumeration of specific angels and ruling on denial

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the obligation to believe in the specific angels mentioned in revelation and the ruling on denying them, including the special case of Munkar and Nakir.

Core Idea
Denying any named angel is disbelief, except Munkar and Nakir.

This passage from al-Bahja al-Saniyya clarifies the doctrinal status of specific angels such as Malik and Ridwan. It emphasizes that denial of these angels constitutes clear disbelief, while offering a nuanced ruling for Munkar and Nakir due to scholarly disagreement. Understanding this distinction is key to proper Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the specific angels named and the ruling stated.
Pay attention to the quote from Shaykh Amin al-Kurdi.
2
Identify the exceptional case
The passage mentions Munkar and Nakir as an exception. Understand why denial of them is fisq, not kufr, due to scholarly disagreement.
This is a subtle but important point in Islamic theology.
3
Reflect on the description of angels
The passage describes angels as honored servants who obey Allah. Consider why this belief is essential for a proper understanding of the unseen world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خازن النار، ورضوان خازن الجنان، وحملة العرش الثمانية. قال شيخنا وشيخ شيوخنا سيدي أمين الكردي رحمه الله تعالى في «تنوير القلوب» (۱): «فمن أنكر وجودهم، أو أنكر واحدًا من هؤلاء المذكورين فهو كافر مخلد في النار ،قطعًا، إلا منكرًا ونكيرًا للخلاف فيهما ، وإنكارهما فسق وليس بكفر. هذا ويجب اعتقاد ما وصفهم الله تعالى به من أنهم: عباد مكرمون، لا يعصون الله ما أمرهم ويفعلون ما يؤمرون. أما ما اشتهر من قصة هاروت وماروت وجعلهما ملكين يعلمان الناس السحر، مع زيادة كذب المؤرخين أنهما عوقبا ومسخا، فذلك كذب وزور وباطل لا يحل اعتقاده ولا سماعه، وإنما الذي يجب اعتقاده فيهما أنه إن لم يكونا ملكين، فالأمر واضح. وإن كانا ملكين فتعليمهما السحر لم يكن لأجل العمل به بل للتحرز منه بتعريف حقيقته، وبيان شره وعقوبته، ولهذا أخبر الله تعالى أنهما ما كانا يعلمان من أحد حتى يقولا: ﴿ إِنَّمَا نَحْنُ فِتْنَةٌ فَلَا تَكْفُر (۲) ، وهذا كتعليم حقيقة الزنا، وأنواع الربا ليتحرز المكلف عنهما، لأن التحرز من الشر موقوف على معرفته، ولهذا قال حذيفة رضي اللَّهُ عَنْهُ: «كان الناس يسألون النبي صَلَّى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ عن الخير، وكنت أسأله عن الشر مخافة أن أقع فيه»(۱)، وقد قيل: عرفت الشَّرَ لا للشر * لكن لتوقيه ومَن لا يعرف الخير * من الشر يقع فيه
Malik is the keeper of the Fire, Ridwan is the keeper of Paradise, and the eight bearers of the Throne. Our shaykh and the shaykh of our shaykhs, Sayyidi Amin al-Kurdi, may Allah have mercy on him, said in 'Tanwir al-Qulub': 'Whoever denies their existence, or denies one of these mentioned, is a disbeliever eternally in the Fire, decisively, except for Munkar and Nakir due to disagreement concerning them; denying them is fisq (immorality) and not disbelief. This and it is obligatory to believe what Allah has described them with: that they are honored servants, they do not disobey Allah in what He commands them and they do what they are commanded. As for what has become famous of the story of Harut and Marut and making them two angels teaching people sorcery, with the added lies of the historians that they were punished and transformed, that is a lie, falsehood, and vanity. It is not permissible to believe it nor to listen to it. Rather, what is obligatory to believe regarding them is that if they were not angels, the matter is clear. And if they were angels, their teaching of sorcery was not for the purpose of practicing it, but to guard against it by defining its reality and explaining its evil and punishment. For this reason, Allah informed that they would not teach anyone until they said, 'We are only a trial, so do not disbelieve.' This is like teaching the reality of adultery and the types of usury so that the morally responsible person may guard against them, because guarding against evil depends on knowing it. For this reason, Hudhayfah, may Allah be pleased with him, said: 'The people used to ask the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, about good, and I would ask him about evil for fear that I might fall into it.' And it has been said: I knew evil not for evil but to guard against it, and whoever does not know good from evil falls into it.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the poem's line about belief in angels and jinn. It first names specific angels: Malik (keeper of Hell), Ridwan (keeper of Paradise), and the eight bearers of the Throne. It then quotes Shaykh Amin al-Kurdi stating that denying any of these angels is clear disbelief (kufr) except for Munkar and Nakir due to scholarly disagreement; denying them is fisq (immorality) but not kufr. Next, it affirms that angels are honored servants who obey Allah completely. Then it addresses the widely circulated story of Harut and Marut, which claims they were two angels who taught people sorcery and were punished and transformed. The author declares this story a lie (kadhib), falsehood (zur), and vanity (batil), and it is neither permissible to believe nor to listen to it. The correct belief is that if they were not angels, the matter is straightforward (they are not angels). If they were angels, their teaching of sorcery was not for practice but for warning—to explain its reality and evil consequences so that people could guard against it. This is similar to teaching the details of forbidden acts like adultery and usury to enable avoidance. The author supports this with the Quranic verse that Harut and Marut would say, 'We are only a trial, so do not disbelieve,' and with the hadith of Hudhayfah who asked about evil to avoid it. Finally, a poetic line is cited: 'I knew evil not for evil but to guard against it, and whoever does not know good from evil falls into it.'

Key Terms
خازن النار
رضوان
حملة العرش
منكر ونكير
فسق
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think denying any angel is always kufr without exception.
Correction: The passage clarifies that denying Munkar and Nakir is fisq, not kufr, due to scholarly disagreement.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on denying the angels Munkar and Nakir?
A) It is disbelief (kufr) that eternally punishes.
B) It is immorality (fisq) but not disbelief.
C) It is permissible because there is disagreement.
D) It is obligatory to deny them.

Key Takeaway
Belief in the specific angels is obligatory, and denial of most is kufr, but the case of Munkar and Nakir is an exception due to scholarly disagreement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe in the angels you have heard of, like the keepers of Hell and Paradise, or do you only pay lip service? How does your daily life reflect this belief?
?
This lesson teaches that even a hint of denial can lead to eternal punishment. Search your heart: are you harboring any doubt about the unseen beings that the Prophet (peace be upon him) informed us of?
?
The exception for Munkar and Nakir shows that not all matters of creed are equally settled. How do you approach areas of legitimate disagreement among scholars? Do you respect the difference or do you judge harshly?

Refuting the Harut and Marut Story
Refutation of the false Harut and Marut story and explanation of the principle of warning against evil

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct Islamic stance on the story of Harut and Marut and the principle that teaching evil can be permissible for warning and prevention purposes.

Core Idea
The Harut and Marut story is a lie; their teaching was a warning, not endorsement.

This passage addresses the widely circulated but false story about the angels Harut and Marut teaching sorcery. It categorically rejects the narrative as a fabrication and provides the correct interpretation: if they were angels, their teaching was to warn against evil, not to promote it. This lesson extracts the key principles for distinguishing truth from falsehood in theological narratives.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage about Harut and Marut
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Note the author's strong condemnation of the story as a lie, falsehood, and vanity.
The passage says it is not permissible to believe or even listen to this story.
2
Understand the alternative interpretation
The passage offers two possibilities: if they were not angels, it's clear; if they were, their teaching was for warning, not practice. Identify the Quranic evidence.
The verse 'We are only a trial, so do not disbelieve' supports this interpretation.
3
Reflect on the principle of knowing evil to avoid it
The analogy with teaching adultery and usury, and the hadith of Hudhayfah, illustrate that guarding against evil requires knowing it. This is a broader theological principle.

خازن النار، ورضوان خازن الجنان، وحملة العرش الثمانية. قال شيخنا وشيخ شيوخنا سيدي أمين الكردي رحمه الله تعالى في «تنوير القلوب» (۱): «فمن أنكر وجودهم، أو أنكر واحدًا من هؤلاء المذكورين فهو كافر مخلد في النار ،قطعًا، إلا منكرًا ونكيرًا للخلاف فيهما ، وإنكارهما فسق وليس بكفر. هذا ويجب اعتقاد ما وصفهم الله تعالى به من أنهم: عباد مكرمون، لا يعصون الله ما أمرهم ويفعلون ما يؤمرون. أما ما اشتهر من قصة هاروت وماروت وجعلهما ملكين يعلمان الناس السحر، مع زيادة كذب المؤرخين أنهما عوقبا ومسخا، فذلك كذب وزور وباطل لا يحل اعتقاده ولا سماعه، وإنما الذي يجب اعتقاده فيهما أنه إن لم يكونا ملكين، فالأمر واضح. وإن كانا ملكين فتعليمهما السحر لم يكن لأجل العمل به بل للتحرز منه بتعريف حقيقته، وبيان شره وعقوبته، ولهذا أخبر الله تعالى أنهما ما كانا يعلمان من أحد حتى يقولا: ﴿ إِنَّمَا نَحْنُ فِتْنَةٌ فَلَا تَكْفُر (۲) ، وهذا كتعليم حقيقة الزنا، وأنواع الربا ليتحرز المكلف عنهما، لأن التحرز من الشر موقوف على معرفته، ولهذا قال حذيفة رضي اللَّهُ عَنْهُ: «كان الناس يسألون النبي صَلَّى اللهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ عن الخير، وكنت أسأله عن الشر مخافة أن أقع فيه»(۱)، وقد قيل: عرفت الشَّرَ لا للشر * لكن لتوقيه ومَن لا يعرف الخير * من الشر يقع فيه
Malik is the keeper of the Fire, Ridwan is the keeper of Paradise, and the eight bearers of the Throne. Our shaykh and the shaykh of our shaykhs, Sayyidi Amin al-Kurdi, may Allah have mercy on him, said in 'Tanwir al-Qulub': 'Whoever denies their existence, or denies one of these mentioned, is a disbeliever eternally in the Fire, decisively, except for Munkar and Nakir due to disagreement concerning them; denying them is fisq (immorality) and not disbelief. This and it is obligatory to believe what Allah has described them with: that they are honored servants, they do not disobey Allah in what He commands them and they do what they are commanded. As for what has become famous of the story of Harut and Marut and making them two angels teaching people sorcery, with the added lies of the historians that they were punished and transformed, that is a lie, falsehood, and vanity. It is not permissible to believe it nor to listen to it. Rather, what is obligatory to believe regarding them is that if they were not angels, the matter is clear. And if they were angels, their teaching of sorcery was not for the purpose of practicing it, but to guard against it by defining its reality and explaining its evil and punishment. For this reason, Allah informed that they would not teach anyone until they said, 'We are only a trial, so do not disbelieve.' This is like teaching the reality of adultery and the types of usury so that the morally responsible person may guard against them, because guarding against evil depends on knowing it. For this reason, Hudhayfah, may Allah be pleased with him, said: 'The people used to ask the Prophet, may Allah bless him and grant him peace, about good, and I would ask him about evil for fear that I might fall into it.' And it has been said: I knew evil not for evil but to guard against it, and whoever does not know good from evil falls into it.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the poem's line about belief in angels and jinn. It first names specific angels: Malik (keeper of Hell), Ridwan (keeper of Paradise), and the eight bearers of the Throne. It then quotes Shaykh Amin al-Kurdi stating that denying any of these angels is clear disbelief (kufr) except for Munkar and Nakir due to scholarly disagreement; denying them is fisq (immorality) but not kufr. Next, it affirms that angels are honored servants who obey Allah completely. Then it addresses the widely circulated story of Harut and Marut, which claims they were two angels who taught people sorcery and were punished and transformed. The author declares this story a lie (kadhib), falsehood (zur), and vanity (batil), and it is neither permissible to believe nor to listen to it. The correct belief is that if they were not angels, the matter is straightforward (they are not angels). If they were angels, their teaching of sorcery was not for practice but for warning—to explain its reality and evil consequences so that people could guard against it. This is similar to teaching the details of forbidden acts like adultery and usury to enable avoidance. The author supports this with the Quranic verse that Harut and Marut would say, 'We are only a trial, so do not disbelieve,' and with the hadith of Hudhayfah who asked about evil to avoid it. Finally, a poetic line is cited: 'I knew evil not for evil but to guard against it, and whoever does not know good from evil falls into it.'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe the story of Harut and Marut teaching sorcery is true.
Correction: The passage declares it a lie and explains that if they taught, it was for warning against sorcery, not for practicing it.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the correct interpretation of Harut and Marut's teaching of sorcery?
A) They taught it for practice and were punished.
B) They taught it to warn and guard against it.
C) They were two humans, not angels.
D) The story is true and must be believed.

Key Takeaway
The story of Harut and Marut is false; if they taught sorcery, it was to warn people and help them avoid it, not to practice it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever accepted a story about religion without verifying it from the Quran and authentic Sunnah? How does the false Harut and Marut narrative challenge you to be more careful about what you believe and spread?
?
If you knew the details of a sin—like sorcery, adultery, or usury—would it make you more likely to fall into it or more likely to avoid it? Examine your heart: are you avoiding knowledge of evil out of fear or out of arrogance?
?
Hudhayfah asked the Prophet specifically about evil to avoid it. What evil in your life are you currently ignorant of because you are too afraid to ask or too lazy to learn? Will you seek that knowledge today?
